(٢) وأخرج ابن عبد البر رحمه الله تعالى عن ابن عون قال: "كنت عند القاسم ابن محمد ) إذ جاءه رجل فسأله عن شيئ فقال القاسم: لا أُحْسِنُه، فجعل الرجل يقول: إنّى دفعت إليك لا أعرف غيرك. فقال القاسم: "لاتنظر إلى طول لحيتى وكثرة الناس حولى، والله ما أُحْسِنه، فقال شيخ من قريش جالس إلى جنبه: يا ابن أخى الزمها، فوالله ما رأيتُك فى مجلس أنبل منك اليوم، فقال القاسم: والله لأن يُقطع لسانى أحب إلى من أن أتكلّم بما لا علم لى به.
Fatwa and its gravity. And from al-Sha'bi, al-Hasan, and Abu al-Husayn (may Allah have mercy on them) they said: 'One of you gives a fatwa on an issue, and if it had been presented to Umar ibn al-Khattab, he would have gathered the people of Badr for it.' And from Sufyan ibn Uyaynah and Sahnun: 'The boldest people in issuing fatwa are the least in knowledge.' And from al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) that he was asked about an issue and did not answer, so it was said to him, he said: 'Until I know whether the merit is in silence or in answering!' And al-Darimi composed a chapter in his Sunan's introduction titled: 'Chapter of those who feared fatwa and disliked extremism and innovation.' And he narrated from Zubaid that he said: 'I never asked Ibrahim (meaning al-Nakha'i) about anything except that I saw disapproval on his face.' And he narrated from Umar ibn Abi Za'idah that he said: 'I have not seen anyone who said more often when asked about something, "I have no knowledge of it" than al-Sha'bi.' And from Ibn Awn that he said: 'When a matter came to al-Sha'bi, he would be cautious, and Ibrahim would say, say, say.' Abu Asim said: 'Al-Sha'bi in this was more excellent in the sight of Ibn Awn than Ibrahim.' And he also narrated from Ja'far ibn Iyas who said: I said to Sa'id ibn Jubayr: Why don't you say anything about divorce? He said: 'There is nothing about it but that I have asked about it, but I dislike to make permissible something forbidden or forbidden something permissible.' And Ibn Abd al-Barr narrated from Ibn Awn who said: 'I was with al-Qasim ibn Muhammad when a man came and asked him about something. Al-Qasim said: I do not know it. The man kept saying: I have come to you and I know no one else. Al-Qasim said: Do not look at the length of my beard and the number of people around me. By Allah, I do not know it. Then an old man from Quraysh sitting beside him said: O son of my brother, stick to it, for by Allah I have not seen you in a gathering nobler than you today. Al-Qasim said: By Allah, I would rather my tongue be cut off than to speak about what I have no knowledge of.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a collection of narrations illustrating the gravity of issuing fatwa (legal opinion) and the extreme caution exercised by the early Muslims. The author begins by citing a report from al-Sha'bi, al-Hasan, and Abu al-Husayn that some people readily give fatwas on issues so momentous that even Caliph Umar would have convened the senior Companions (the people of Badr) to decide them. This emphasizes that not every qualified person should rush to answer. Then a saying of Sufyan ibn Uyaynah and Sahnun follows: the boldest in fatwa are the least knowledgeable—implying that true knowledge breeds caution. The great scholar al-Shafi'i is reported to have refrained from answering a question, saying he needed to weigh whether silence or speech was more virtuous in that case. Imam al-Darimi dedicated a chapter in his Sunan to those who feared fatwa and disliked extremism (tanaTTu') and innovation (tabaddud). The narrations that follow illustrate this: Zubaid noted that when he asked Ibrahim al-Nakha'i about anything, his face showed disapproval—indicating the burden of giving answers. Umar ibn Abi Za'idah said he never saw anyone more ready to say 'I do not know' than al-Sha'bi. Ibn Awn compared al-Sha'bi and Ibrahim, noting that al-Sha'bi was more cautious. Ja'far ibn Iyas asked Sa'id ibn Jubayr why he remained silent on divorce (a sensitive area). Sa'id replied that he had studied it thoroughly but feared to unlawfully permit or forbid something—so he hedged. Finally, a powerful story about al-Qasim ibn Muhammad: a man sought his fatwa, insisting he knew no one else. Al-Qasim humbly admitted ignorance despite the man's flattery, and swore he would rather lose his tongue than speak without knowledge. An older onlooker praised al-Qasim's noble restraint. Collectively, these anecdotes teach that fatwa is a serious responsibility requiring deep knowledge and fear of Allah; the safest course is often reticence. The Hanafi school upholds this caution: a mufti must be well-versed in the Quran, Sunnah, ijma, and qiyas, and should not issue opinions carelessly.

Key Terms
الفتوى (al-fatwā): A non-binding legal opinion issued by a qualified scholar on a matter of Islamic law.
التنطع (al-tanaṭṭuʿ): Going to extremes in religious matters, often leading to excessive questioning or strictness.
التبدع (al-tabaddud): Innovation in religion, introducing practices not established by the Shariah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did al-Qasim ibn Muhammad say when a man insisted he give a fatwa?
A) 'I will look into it and return.'
B) 'I do not know it, and I would rather my tongue be cut off than speak without knowledge.'
C) 'Come back tomorrow.'
D) 'You are right; I will answer.'

Key Takeaway
The greatest scholars were the most cautious about issuing fatwas, often preferring silence over risky answers.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

The Gravity of Fatwa: Imam Malik's Caution
Narrates Imam Malik's extreme caution in issuing fatwas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key adab of a mufti as exemplified by Imam Malik and explain why saying 'Lā adrī' is a fundamental principle of responsible iftāʾ.

Core Idea
A mufti's fear of Allah outweighs his desire to answer—saying 'Lā adrī' is a shield against sin.

Muftis today face immense pressure to provide instant answers, but classical scholars like Imam Malik remind us of the gravity of this role. This passage from 'Uṣūl al-Iftāʾ wa Ādābuh' presents vivid anecdotes showing Malik's extreme caution, fear of accountability, and reliance on the phrase 'Lā adrī' (I do not know). These lessons are as vital for modern muftis as they were centuries ago.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the gravity of fatwa
The passage opens by stating 'The fatwa and its gravity.' This sets the tone: issuing a fatwa is a serious responsibility with weighty consequences.
Fatwa is not merely giving an opinion; it is speaking on behalf of Allah and His religion.
2
Observe Imam Malik's physical and emotional state when asked
When questioned, Malik would change color, bow his head, move his lips in dhikr, and sometimes remain silent. This shows his profound taqwa and awareness of the responsibility.
His hesitation was not due to ignorance but fear of misguiding someone.
3
Analyze his use of 'Lā adrī' 'I do not know'
Malik frequently said 'I do not know' even when the questioner traveled far. He considered it a bequest to his students and a foundation to which they should resort.
Saying 'Lā adrī' is praiseworthy and sometimes obligatory if knowledge is lacking.
4
Understand the principle: a mufti must save himself first
Malik said: 'Do you want to make me a proof between you and Allah? I need first to see how I can save myself, then I will save you.' This highlights personal accountability before giving guidance.
The mufti's own salvation is prioritized; giving a wrong fatwa endangers both.

Fatwa and its gravity. He left until I look into it. So he left and was hesitant in it. We said to him about that, so he wept and said: 'Indeed I fear that I will have from the questioner a day and what a day!' He said and added: 'When Malik sat, he would bow his head, move his lips with the remembrance of Allah, and not turn right or left. When he was asked about a matter, his color would change—he was red with yellowness—so he would turn yellow, bow his head, and move his lips. Then he would say: "Whatever Allah wills, and there is no power except with Allah." Sometimes he was asked about fifty matters and would not answer any one of them.' And some said: 'It is as if Malik, by Allah, when asked about a matter, is standing between Paradise and Hell.' And Musa ibn Dawud said: 'I did not see any of the scholars who said "How excellent" more than Malik.' And Ibn Mahdi said: A man asked Malik about a matter, mentioning that he was sent about it from a journey of six months from the Maghrib. He said to him: 'Inform the one who sent you that I have no knowledge of it.' He said: 'Who knows it?' He said: 'The one whom Allah taught.' And a man asked him about a matter that the people of the Maghrib had entrusted to him, so he said: 'I do not know! We have not been tested with this matter in our land, nor have we heard any of our shaykhs speak about it, but return.' When it was the next day, he came carrying his load on a mule, leading it, and said: 'My matter!' He said: 'I do not know what it is.' The man said: 'O Abu Abdullah, I left behind me someone who says there is no one on the face of the earth more knowledgeable than you.' So Malik said, without being dismayed: 'When you return, inform them that I do not know.' And another asked him: 'O Abu Abdullah, answer me.' He said: 'Woe to you, do you want to make me a proof between you and Allah? I need first to see how I can save myself, then I will save you.' Ibn Abi Hazim said: Malik said: 'If someone asks you about a matter, begin with yourself and safeguard it.' And Khalid ibn Khirash said: 'I came from Iraq to Malik with forty matters, and he answered me only five of them.' And Malik said: I heard Ibn Hurmuz say: 'It is befitting that a scholar bequeath to his companions the statement "I do not know" so that it becomes a foundation in their hands to which they resort. If any of them is asked about what is not transmitted, he says: "I do not know."' And Ibn Wahb (1) said: 'Malik used to say about most of what he was asked: "I do not know."' Umar ibn Yazid said: I said to Malik about that, so he said: 'The people of Syria will return to their Syria, the people of Iraq to their Iraq, and the people of Egypt to their Egypt, then perhaps I will retract what I gave them as fatwa.' He said: 'So I informed al-Layth about that, and he wept and said: "Malik, by Allah, is stronger than al-Layth" or similar.' Ibn Wahb said: I asked Malik about thirty thousand novel issues in his lifetime, and he said about a third or half or as Allah willed of them: 'I do not know, I do not know.' And some said: 'When you, O Abu Abdullah, say "I do not know," then who knows?' He said: 'Woe to you, do you not know me? What am I? What is my status that I should know what you do not know?' Then he began to argue with the hadith of Ibn Umar, saying: 'I do not know.' So who am I? Indeed, people are destroyed by self-admiration and seeking leadership. (2) al-Layth... The Imams. (1) Imam Ibn Wahb... (biographical footnote) ... etc.

Commentary
This passage is a mufti_adab text from the book 'Uṣūl al-Iftā' wa Ādābuh' by Muḥammad Taqī al-ʿUthmānī. It presents multiple anecdotes illustrating the profound fear and caution of Imām Mālik ibn Anas when it comes to issuing fatwas. The opening line sets the theme: 'The fatwa and its gravity.' The author demonstrates through these stories that a true scholar does not take the responsibility of giving fatwa lightly. Imām Mālik would physically show signs of anxiety (change of color, lowering head, moving lips in dhikr) when asked a question. He would often refuse to answer, even if the questioner traveled far. He emphasized the importance of saying 'Lā adrī' (I do not know) and considered it a foundational principle for students. The passage also narrates his fear that he might have to retract his fatwa later, and his concern for his own salvation before that of the questioner. The footnotes provide brief biographies of Ibn Wahb and al-Layth ibn Saʿd. For a Hanafi mufti, these lessons are directly applicable: the gravity of fatwa, humility, and the courage to say 'I do not know' are essential traits of a responsible mufti.

Key Terms
لا أدري (lā adrī): A phrase meaning 'I do not know,' used by scholars when they lack knowledge about a particular issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Imam Malik say when a man came from a six-month journey asking a question?
A) He answered immediately to honor the traveler.
B) He said 'I do not know' and refused to answer.
C) He gave a lengthy answer.
D) He asked for a fee.

Key Takeaway
Imam Malik's example teaches that a mufti must prioritize taqwa over reputation and never hesitate to say 'I do not know' when uncertain.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

Saying 'I Don't Know': The Mufti's First Principle
Presenting anecdotes showing the extreme caution of Imam Malik in issuing fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principle of saying 'I don't know' as a core mufti adab, explain its rationale using Malik's practice, and apply this adab in evaluating a mufti's conduct.

Core Idea
Saying 'I don't know' is a mufti's shield; pride in knowledge destroys its value.

This lesson takes you into the heart of mufti adab through the vivid stories of Imam Malik and his contemporaries. You will see how the greatest scholars feared the responsibility of issuing a fatwa, and how they made 'I don't know' a guiding principle. These accounts are not mere history—they set the standard for every mufti's character.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Read the opening report by Ibn Hurmuz: what key phrase should become 'a principle in their hands' for the scholar's companions?
This principle is about training the next generation to be cautious.
2
Examine Malik's practice
How often did Imam Malik say 'I don't know'? Count occurrences in the reports. Why did he say it so frequently?
His reluctance was not ignorance but awareness of the weight of fatwa.
3
Understand the rationale
Malik explained that even after giving a fatwa, the people return to their lands and he might later retract. What fear does this express?
The mufti's accountability before Allah is paramount.
4
Recognize the virtue
Malik rebukes a questioner who calls a question 'light', citing Quran 73:5. He weeps thinking his words will be carried far. How do these emotions reinforce the gravity of fatwa?
The mufti's words are recorded; he must be constantly conscious.
5
Apply the principle
Consider a modern mufti asked about an unfamiliar financial transaction. How should he respond based on this lesson?
Saying 'I don't know' is preferable to risking error.

" وقال ابن وهب (۱). "كان مالك يقول في أكثر ما يُسأل عنه منه لا أدري." قال عمر بن يزيد فقلت لمالك في ذلك، فقال: "يرجع أهل الشام إلى شامهم وأهل العراق إلى عراقهم وأهل مصر إلى مصرهم، ثم لعلى أرجع عمّا أفتيتهم به. " قال: "فأخبرت بذلك الليث) فبكى، وقال: "مالك" والله أقوى من الليث أو نحو هذا." قال ابن وهب: سألت مالكاً في ثلاثين ألف مسألةٍ نوازل فى عمره فقال في ثلثها أو شطرها أو ما شاء الله منها: "لا أحسن، ولا أدرى " وقال بعضهم: "إذا قلت أنت يا أبا عبد الله "لا أدرى " فمن يدري؟ قال: "ويحك ما عرفتني؟ وما أنا؟ وأي شيئ منزلتي حتى أدري ما لا تَدْرُون. ثم أخذ يحتج بحديث ابن ،عمر، يقول: "لا أدري " فمن أنا؟ وإنّما أهلك الناس العُجب وطلب الرئاسة.

" وكان السائل ذا قدر، فغضب مالك وقال: "مسألة خفيفة سهلة؟ ليس في العلم شيء خفيف. أما سمعت قول الله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّا سَنُلْقِي عَلَيْكَ قَوْلاً ثَقِيلاً ﴾ [المزمل : ٥] فالعلم كله ثقيل، وبخاصة ما يُسأل عنه يوم القيامة." وقال له ابن القاسم: ليس" بعد أهل المدينة أعلم بالبيوع من أهل مصر." فقال مالك: "ومن أين علموها؟ " قال: "منك." قال مالك: "ما أعلمها أنا، فكيف يعلمونها؟ " وقال القَعْنَبي: "دخلت على مالك فوجدته باكيا، فسألته عن ذلك فقال: "ومن أحق بالبكاء مني ؟ لا أتكلم بكلمةٍ إلا كتبت بالأقلام وحملت إلى الآفاق (9) وقال سُحْنُون رحمه الله تعالى: "إنّي لأسأل مسألة فأعرف فى أى كتاب وورقة وصفحة وسطر، فما يمنعنى عن الجواب فيها إلا كراهة الجرأة بعدى على الفتيا. (۳) الإمام الماوردي الشافعي: علي بن محمد بن حبيب الإمام أبو الحسن الماوردي، نسبة إلى عمل عائلته بصناعة ماء الورد وبيعه. هو صاحب "الحاوى" و "أدب الدنيا الدين " و "النكت" في
التفسير ودلائل النبوة" و"الأحكام السلطانية" وغيرها. تفقه بالبصرة على الإمام الصيمري رحل إلى الإمام أبي حامد الإسفرايني ببغداد رحمهم الله تعالى. وكانت له اليد الباسطة في المذهب والتفنن التام في سائر العلوم. وقد نبه الحافظ ابن الصلاح رحمه الله أنه كان يذهب إلى مذهب الاعتزال في بعض المسائل، يبوح بها فى تفسيره، فليتفطّن لذلك. تُوُفِّي رحمه الله سلخ شهر ربيع الأول سنة ٤٥٠ هـ عن ست وثمانين سنة. ملخص من طبقات الشافعية الكبرى ٥: ٢٦٧ وما بعدها، وسير أعلام النبلاء ١٨: ٦٤ إلى (٦٨
With forty questions, he only answered me on five. And Malik said: I heard Ibn Hurmuz say: 'A scholar should bequeath to his companions the statement "I don't know" so that it becomes a principle in their hands to which they resort. When any of them is asked about what he does not narrate, he says: "I don't know."' And Ibn Wahb said: 'Malik used to say, regarding most of what he was asked about, "I don't know."' Umar ibn Yazid said: I said to Malik about that, and he said: 'The people of Syria return to their Syria, the people of Iraq to their Iraq, and the people of Egypt to their Egypt. Then perhaps I retract what I gave them as fatwa.' He said: 'I informed al-Layth about that, and he wept and said: "Malik, by Allah, is stronger than al-Layth," or similar to this.' Ibn Wahb said: I asked Malik about thirty thousand novel issues in his lifetime, and he said about a third or half or as Allah willed of them: 'I do not know, and I do not know.' And someone said: 'When you, O Abu Abdullah, say "I don't know," then who knows?' He said: 'Woe to you, do you not know me? What am I? What is my status that I should know what you do not know?' Then he began to argue with the hadith of Ibn Umar, saying: 'He said "I don't know," so who am I? Indeed, people are destroyed by self-admiration and desire for leadership.'

(1) Imam Ibn Wahb: Abdullah ibn Wahb ibn Muslim al-Fihri by allegiance, the Egyptian, Abu Muhammad, a jurist from the companions of Imam Malik. He combined jurisprudence, hadith, and worship. He has books, including 'al-Jami' in hadith and 'al-Muwatta' in hadith. He was a memorizer, trustworthy, and a mujtahid. He was offered the judgeship but hid himself and stayed in his house. He was born in 125 AH and died in 197 AH in Egypt. Thus in al-A'lam with slight modification 4:144.

(2) Al-Layth ibn Sa'd: Imam of the Egyptian lands in fiqh and hadith, he was its scholar and chief. Al-Hafiz al-Dhahabi narrated from Ishaq ibn Ismail al-Ramli, may Allah have mercy on them, who said: I heard Muhammad ibn Ramh say: 'The income of ibn Sa'd each year was eighty thousand dinars, and Allah did not oblige upon him any zakat of a dirham at all.' And Shams al-Din ibn Khallakan said in al-Wafayat: 'I saw in some collections that al-Layth was of the Hanafi school.' And Allama al-Qurashi mentioned him in Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya. He died, may Allah have mercy on him, on Friday night, the 15th of Sha'ban, 175 AH, at the age of 81. Summarized from Tadhkirat al-Huffaz 2:224-226, Siyar A'lam al-Nubala 8:152, Wafayat al-A'yan 4:127, and al-Jawahir al-Mudi'a 2:720.

'And this will vanish shortly.' And Mus'ab said: Malik was asked about an issue and he said: 'I don't know.' The questioner said to him: 'It is a light and easy issue, and I only wanted to inform the governor through it.' And the questioner was of high status, so Malik became angry and said: 'A light and easy issue? There is nothing light in knowledge. Have you not heard the saying of Allah Almighty: "Indeed, We will cast upon you a heavy word" [al-Muzzammil: 5]? So all knowledge is heavy, especially what one is asked about on the Day of Resurrection.' And Ibn al-Qasim said to him: 'There is no one after the people of Madinah more knowledgeable about buying and selling than the people of Egypt.' Malik said: 'And how did they learn it?' He said: 'From you.' Malik said: 'I do not know it, so how can they know it?' And al-Qa'nabi said: 'I entered upon Malik and found him weeping, so I asked him about that, and he said: 'Who is more deserving of weeping than me? I do not utter a word except it is written with pens and carried to distant horizons.' And Sahnun, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'I am asked an issue, and I know in which book, page, and line it is, yet nothing prevents me from answering except dislike of boldness in giving fatwa.' And Allama al-Mawardi al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, says in his book Adab al-Din wa al-Dunya: 'Among what I warn you about...'

(3) Imam al-Mawardi al-Shafi'i: Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Habib, Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Mawardi, attributed to his family's work in making rose water and selling it. He is the author of al-Hawi, Adab al-Dunya wa al-Din, al-Nukat in tafsir, Dala'il al-Nubuwwa, al-Ahkam al-Sultaniyya, and others. He studied fiqh in Basra under Imam al-Saymari and traveled to Imam Abu Hamid al-Isfara'ini in Baghdad, may Allah have mercy on them. He had extensive knowledge in the madhhab and complete mastery of all sciences. Al-Hafiz Ibn al-Salah, may Allah have mercy on him, noted that he leaned toward the Mu'tazili school in some issues, which he disclosed in his tafsir; so one should be aware of that. He died, may Allah have mercy on him, at the end of Rabi' al-Awwal 450 AH at the age of 86. Summarized from Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya al-Kubra 5:267ff and Siyar A'lam al-Nubala 18:64-68.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a collection of historical reports and sayings illustrating the profound caution and fear of responsibility that characterized early scholars, particularly Imam Malik, regarding the issuance of fatwa. The author uses these anecdotes to impress upon the reader the gravity of speaking on behalf of Allah's law. The text begins with Malik reporting Ibn Hurmuz's advice that a scholar should teach his students to say 'I don't know,' making it a foundational principle. Malik himself would often say 'I don't know' to most questions, demonstrating his humility. When questioned about this, he explained that even if he gives a fatwa, the people will return to their lands, and he might later retract his opinion—a sobering recognition of his fallibility. The story of al-Layth weeping upon hearing this underscores the emotional weight of the mufti's task. Ibn Wahb's report that Malik answered only about a third of thirty thousand questions with 'I don't know' or similar shows his reluctance. A challenger asked Malik who would know if he didn't, and Malik replied that he was not so great, citing the example of Ibn Umar who also said 'I don't know.' He then warns that self-admiration and love of leadership ruin people. The second part continues with more examples: Malik rebukes a questioner who called a question 'light,' citing the Quranic verse that knowledge is heavy, especially what one will be asked about on Judgment Day. He also denies knowledge of sales despite being the source of Egyptian knowledge. Malik weeps knowing his words are recorded and spread. Sahnun admits he knows a ruling perfectly but refrains due to fear of boldness. Finally, al-Mawardi is quoted cautioning about something (text cuts off). The footnotes provide brief biographies of Ibn Wahb, al-Layth ibn Sa'd, and al-Mawardi, giving context to the narrators. The overall lesson is that a mufti must approach his task with utmost humility and fear, never taking it lightly.

Key Terms
فتوى (fatwa): A non-binding legal opinion issued by a qualified scholar (mufti) in response to a question.
لا أدري (la adri): An expression signifying lack of knowledge, used by scholars to decline answering when uncertain.
مسألة (mas'ala): A juridical issue or question presented to a mufti for a fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was Imam Malik's response when a questioner described a question as 'light and easy'?
A) He answered the question immediately.
B) He said there is nothing light in knowledge and quoted Quran 73:5.
C) He referred the questioner to another scholar.
D) He laughed and said the questioner was mistaken.

Key Takeaway
A mufti's first duty is to know his limits; saying 'I don't know' is an act of worship and protection.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
intermediate
8 min
Lessons in Mufti Adab: The Gravity of Fatwa
Warnings and stories on the gravity of fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the key ethical principles for a mufti from the warnings of Imam Malik, Sahnun, and al-Mawardi, and apply them to modern fatwa scenarios.

Core Idea
Issuing a fatwa is a weighty responsibility; even the greatest scholars feared the accountability it entails.

This lesson presents a collection of powerful narratives from the early masters of fiqh and fatwa. These stories are not mere history; they are foundational to the adab al-mufti tradition. By reflecting on them, we internalize the gravity of the task before us and learn the humility required to speak in Allah's name.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common theme
Read through all the anecdotes. What single attitude unites the responses of Malik, Sahnun, and al-Mawardi? Look for words like 'heavy', 'weeping', 'fear', 'humbling'.
Focus on how each scholar reacts to the burden of fatwa.
2
Analyze Malik's reply to the 'light' question
Examine Malik's anger at the questioner calling the question 'light and easy'. How does he use Quran 73:5 to refute that? What does this tell you about the nature of all religious knowledge?
Malik says 'there is nothing light in knowledge' – find the key phrase.
3
Examine Malik's weeping and Sahnun's hesitation
Why does Malik weep? Why does Sahnun refrain from answering even when he knows the exact location of the answer? What does this reveal about their sense of accountability?
Note Malik's statement: every word is written and carried to the horizons.
4
Dissect al-Mawardi's personal experience
Al-Mawardi wrote a comprehensive book on sales. What was his initial feeling of pride? How did the two Bedouins' question humble him? What lesson does he draw from the contrasting swift answer of his colleague?
The lesson is about pride and the limits of one's knowledge.
5
Synthesize the ethical code for the mufti
From these stories, extract a set of concrete attitudes a mufti must adopt. List at least three principles, each supported by a specific anecdote from this passage.
Think about humility, caution, readiness to say 'I do not know', and the fear of accountability.

Principles of Fatwa
The Fatwa and Its Gravity
And this will fade shortly." And Mus'ab said: Malik was asked a question and said: 'I do not know.' The questioner said: 'It is a light, easy question, and I only wanted to inform the ruler (through it).' The questioner was of status, so Malik became angry and said: 'A light, easy question? There is nothing light in knowledge. Have you not heard Allah's saying: "Indeed, We will cast upon you a heavy word" (al-Muzzammil: 5)? All knowledge is heavy, especially that which one will be asked about on the Day of Judgment.' And Ibn al-Qasim said to him: 'There is no one after the people of Medina more knowledgeable about sales than the people of Egypt.' Malik said: 'From where did they learn it?' He said: 'From you.' Malik said: 'I do not know it myself, so how can they know it?' And al-Qa'nabi said: 'I entered upon Malik and found him weeping, so I asked him about that. He said: "And who is more deserving of weeping than I? Whenever I speak a word, it is written down with pens and carried to the horizons."' And Sahnun, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Indeed, I am asked a question, and I know in which book, leaf, page, and line (the answer is), but nothing prevents me from answering except the dislike of being audacious after me in giving fatwa.' And al-Mawardi al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, says in his book 'Adab al-Dunya wa al-Din': 'Among what I warn you about is my condition: I authored a book on sales, collecting what I could from people's books, exerting myself and toiling my mind until it became refined and complete, and I almost admired it and imagined that I was the most knowledgeable of people in its science. Then, while I was in my gathering, two Bedouins came to me and asked me about a sale they had contracted in the desert with conditions that included four issues, and I did not know the answer to any of them. So I lowered my head in thought, reflecting on my state and their state. They said: "Do you have no answer to what we asked, while you are the leader of this group?" I said: "No." They said: "It is yours" and left. Then they came to someone who was much more advanced in knowledge than many of my companions, and they asked him, and he answered them quickly to their satisfaction. They left pleased with his answer, praising his knowledge. And that became a restraining advice and a warning of admonition, humbling the leadership of the soul and lowering the wing of pride.'
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage from 'Usul al-Ifta wa Adabuhu' presents a collection of narratives from early scholars—especially Imam Malik and Imam al-Mawardi—that serve as a powerful warning about the gravity of issuing fatwas. The text falls under the category of mufti adab (ethics of the mufti). It opens with Imam Malik's well-known refusal to answer a question he did not know, and his anger when the questioner belittled the burden of knowledge. Malik cites the Quranic verse about the 'heavy word' to emphasize that all religious knowledge carries weight, especially that for which one is accountable. The anecdotes continue: Malik disclaims expertise in sales (buyu'), even when told others learned from him. He is found weeping, saying every word he speaks is recorded and spread. Sahnun confesses that even when he knows the exact location of an answer in a book, he refrains from answering out of fear of being audacious in fatwa. Finally, al-Mawardi (a Shafi'i scholar) narrates a humbling personal experience: after writing a comprehensive book on sales, two Bedouins asked him about a specific contract involving four issues, and he could not answer, while a less prominent colleague answered promptly. This taught him humility and shattered his pride. The lesson for the mufti is clear: fatwa is a serious responsibility; one must never be overconfident, and must always fear falling short. The stories are linked by the theme of caution, humility, and the weight of speaking in Allah's name.

Key Terms
الفتوى (al-fatwa): A non-binding legal opinion issued by a qualified jurist (mufti) in response to a specific question.
خطورة الفتوى (khuturat al-fatwa): The grave responsibility and potential consequences of issuing a fatwa, especially the accountability before Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Imam Malik become angry when the questioner described the question as 'light and easy'?
A) Because the questioner was of low status and should not ask questions.
B) Because no knowledge is light; all religious knowledge is heavy and carries accountability.
C) Because the question was actually very difficult and the questioner was lying.
D) Because Malik did not like being interrupted in his studies.

Key Takeaway
The stories of early scholars teach that giving fatwa requires profound humility, constant fear of accountability, and readiness to admit ignorance.

The Severity of Fatwa and Humility of the Mufti
Story of a jurist humbled by Bedouins' inquiry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key adab of the mufti demonstrated in the story and apply the lesson of humility to your own practice of issuing fatwa.

Core Idea
Pride in knowledge blinds a mufti; humility opens the door to true understanding.

This passage presents a powerful story from the life of a great scholar (likely Imam al-Ghazali) who, after compiling a comprehensive book on sales, was humbled by two Bedouins with a practical question he could not answer. The story emphasizes that theoretical mastery is no substitute for the humility required in issuing fatwa. As a mufti, one must never assume complete knowledge, for the simplest question may expose gaps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the protagonist's initial state
Read the opening lines describing the jurist's feeling after writing his book. He almost became impressed with it and imagined himself as the most knowledgeable.
This sets up the danger of pride (al-ujb).
2
Recognize the challenge
Two Bedouins ask about a sale they contracted with four specific conditions. The jurist could not answer any of them.
The question was practical, not theoretical.
3
Observe the humility lesson
The jurist lowered his head, pondered, and reflected on his state and theirs. He admitted 'No.' This act of admitting ignorance humbled him.
Admission of ignorance is a sign of humility, not failure.
4
Contrast with the other scholar
A less learned person answered quickly and satisfactorily, leaving the Bedouins pleased. This shows that knowledge alone is not enough; humility and readiness to learn are crucial.
The quicker answer didn't come from greater knowledge but from practical readiness.
5
Derive the adab
The jurist took the encounter as a prohibitive admonition. The key takeaway: a mufti must always remain humble, never assume he knows everything, and be open to learning from any source.
This adab is foundational for issuing sound fatwa.

الفتوى وخطورتها

من حالى أنى صنفت فى البيوع كتاباً جمعته ما استطعت من كتب الناس، وأجْهَدتُ فيه نفسى وكَدَدت فيه خاطرى، حتى إذا تهذب واستكمل وكِدتْ أُعجب به وتصورت أنّى أشد الناس اطلاعاً بعلمه، حضرني وأنا في مجلسي أعرابيان فسألاني عن بيع عقداه في البادية على شروط تضمنت أربع مسائل، ولم أعرف لشيئ منها جواباً، فأطرقت مفكرا، وبحالي وحالهما معتبرا، فقالا: "أما عندك فيما سألناك جواب، وأنت زعيم هذه الجماعة؟ فقلت: "لا" فقالا: "إنها لك. " وانصرفا، ثم أتيا من قد يتقدمه في العلم كثير من أصحابي، فسألاه فأجابهما مسرعاً بما أقنعهما، فانصرفا عنه راضيين بجوابه حامدين لعلمه... فكان ذلك زاجر نصيحةٍ ونذيرَ عِظَةٍ تذلَّلَ لهما قياد النفس، وانخفض لهما جناح العُجب.

مناهج الفتوى
فى السلف
Principles of Issuing Fatwa 27 The Fatwa and its Gravity. It is related that I authored a book on sales, compiling what I could from the works of people, exerting myself and taxing my mind until it was refined and completed. I almost became impressed with it and imagined that I was the most knowledgeable of people in that field. Then two Bedouins came to me while I was in my gathering and asked me about a sale they had contracted in the desert on conditions that included four issues. I did not know the answer to any of them. So I lowered my head in thought, reflecting on my state and their state. They said: 'Do you have no answer to what we asked, while you are the leader of this group?' I said: 'No.' They said: 'It is yours (i.e., the ignorance).' Then they left. Then they went to someone who was less knowledgeable than many of my companions, and they asked him. He answered them quickly with what satisfied them. They left him pleased with his answer, praising his knowledge. That was a prohibitive admonition and a warning lesson, through which the reins of the ego became humbled and the wing of pride was lowered. (1) M. (1) Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya al-Kubra, Fourth Class, regarding one who died between 400 and 500 AH - (5/269) Methodologies of Fatwa among the Salaf.

Commentary
This passage presents a narrative from the life of a prominent jurist (likely Imam al-Ghazali, referenced in the footnote) to illustrate the gravity of issuing fatwa and the necessity of humility for a mufti. The author describes how, after compiling a comprehensive book on sales and feeling pride in his achievement, two Bedouins approached him with a practical question on a sale contract involving four legal issues. Despite his extensive knowledge, he could not answer. The Bedouins expressed disappointment, saying 'It is yours' — implying that the shame of ignorance belonged to him. They then consulted a less learned person who answered promptly and to their satisfaction. The jurist took this as a severe admonition, which humbled his ego and crushed his pride. The lesson is that a mufti must never rely solely on theoretical knowledge or become arrogant; even simple questions can expose gaps in understanding. The footnote traces the story to the Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya al-Kubra, confirming its historical authenticity. The final line 'Methodologies of Fatwa among the Salaf' marks the beginning of a new section, but the core lesson of the story remains: the responsibility of fatwa demands constant humility and self-reflection.

Key Terms
الفتوى (al-fatwa): A legal response to a question on Islamic law, issued by a qualified mufti.
العجب (al-'ujb): Self-admiration or pride in one's knowledge, which the author warns against as a dangerous trait for a mufti.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Fatwa in the Prophet's Era: Delegation and Training
Historical overview of fatwa in the Prophet's era with two illustrative hadiths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two prophetic hadiths on delegation of ifta and explain how they establish the principle of training in ijtihad within the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
The Prophet was the first mufti, but he delegated to companions to train them in ijtihad, legitimizing qualified independent reasoning.

This passage from Mufti Taqi Usmani's 'Principles of Ifta' traces the origins of fatwa to the Prophet himself. It highlights that while the Prophet was the sole source of divine rulings, he actively prepared his Companions for the role of mufti by delegating cases. Understanding this early model is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to ifta, which balances textual authority with structured ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary mufti and his source
The Prophet Muhammad (SAW) was the first mufti, issuing fatwas based on direct revelation (wahy).
2
Note the delegation practice
Although he was the sole mufti, he occasionally assigned ifta or qada to certain Companions to train them.
3
Analyze the first hadith (Amr ibn al-As)
The Prophet promised ten rewards for correct judgment and one reward for incorrect judgment after ijtihad, indicating that sincere effort is valuable.
4
Analyze the second hadith (Ma'qil al-Muzani)
The Prophet assured that Allah is with the judge as long as he does not intentionally oppress, encouraging ijtihad without fear of sin.
5
Derive the principle of training in ijtihad
These hadiths establish that training in ijtihad is a prophetic sunnah and that a mufti is rewarded for sincere effort, which shapes Hanafi adab al-mufti.

Methodologies of Fatwa
In the Early Period

Principles of Ifta
29
Methodologies of Fatwa in the Early Period
The Fatwa in the Era of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace
The first to undertake the position of ifta was the Master of the Messengers and the Seal of the Prophets, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. He used to give fatwas from Allah, the Exalted and Majestic, by His clear revelation. His fatwas, peace be upon him, were comprehensive rulings, and they are the greatest source of Islamic law after the Holy Quran. The Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, preserved them in their hearts and in writings, as established in the discussion of compilation and writing of Hadith. No one else in the era of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, occupied himself with the position of ifta besides him. However, he, peace be upon him, sometimes delegated the matter of ifta or qada to some of his Companions, perhaps to train them in ijtihad and deduction, such as what al-Hakim narrated from Abdullah ibn Amr, may Allah be pleased with them both: "Two men referred their dispute to the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and he said to Amr: 'Judge between them.' He said: 'Shall I judge between them while you are present, O Messenger of Allah?' He said: 'Yes, and if you are correct, you will have ten rewards, and if you exert effort and err, you will have one reward.'" And similar to what was narrated from Ma'qil al-Muzani, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: "The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, ordered me to judge between a people, so I said: 'I do not know how to judge, O Messenger of Allah!' He said: 'Allah is with the judge as long as he does not intentionally oppress.'" And likewise, the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, sent...
(1) Mustadrak al-Hakim 499, he said: Its chain is sound, and they did not narrate it with this wording. Al-Dhahabi said: Farj ibn Fadalah is weak.
(2) Narrated by Ahmad in his Musnad 33:420 no. 20305, and in its chain is Tufi' ibn al-Harith, who is Abu Dawud al-A'ma, abandoned in hadith. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage discusses the early methodology of fatwa during the lifetime of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). It affirms that the Prophet himself was the first and primary mufti, receiving revelation from Allah. His fatwas are considered a primary source of Islamic law after the Quran. The Companions preserved these fatwas by memory and writing. Although no one else formally held the position of mufti during the Prophet's time, he occasionally delegated the task of giving rulings or judging to certain Companions, not because he was unavailable, but to train them in ijtihad (independent reasoning) and istinbat (deduction of rulings). The passage cites two narrations as evidence: one where the Prophet told Amr ibn al-As that a judge who is correct gets ten rewards, and if he errs after striving, he gets one reward. The second narration consoles Ma'qil al-Muzani, who felt inadequate to judge, by reminding him that Allah is with the judge as long as he does not oppress intentionally. These instances illustrate the early training of Companions in legal reasoning and the encouragement of ijtihad within the framework of divine guidance.

Key Terms
الإفتاء (al-ifta): The act of issuing a fatwa; the position or function of giving formal legal opinions in Islam.
الفتوى (al-fatwa): A non-binding legal opinion issued by a qualified scholar (mufti) on a specific question.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): The process of exerting one's utmost effort to derive a legal ruling from the primary sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he was too busy to handle all cases
B) To train them in ijtihad and deduction
C) Because he was not present in all regions
D) To test their obedience

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's delegation of ifta to companions established the principle of training in ijtihad, which is foundational for the Hanafi approach to fatwa.

The Prophet's Foundation of Ifta and Ijtihad
Historical origins of ifta and delegation to companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Prophet established the office of ifta and trained companions through delegation, and evaluate the authenticity of the Mu'adh hadith as a proof for ijtihad.

Core Idea
The Prophet was the first mufti; he trained companions by delegating and approving their ijtihad.

This lesson traces the beginnings of ifta (fatwa-issuing) back to the Prophet himself. You will learn that the Prophet was the primary source of fatwa during his lifetime, but he also systematically trained his companions by delegating them to judge and issue fatwas. The famous hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal outlines the methodology of deriving rulings, which later became the foundation of Hanafi usul. We will also examine how scholars defended this hadith despite some chain criticisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary office-bearer of ifta
State that the Prophet was the first and chief mufti, receiving revelation directly. His fatwas are second only to the Quran as a source of law.
This establishes the highest authority for fatwa.
2
Analyze the Prophet's delegation to companions
The Prophet delegated some companions for qada and ifta as training. Identify the three examples: 'Amr ibn al-'As, Ma'qil al-Muzani, and Mu'adh ibn Jabal.
The delegation was deliberate to practice ijtihad.
3
Extract the methodology from Mu'adh's hadith
Mu'adh outlined a clear priority: Quran, then Sunnah, then ijtihad. The Prophet approved this sequence.
This is a foundational methodology for Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
4
Evaluate the chain criticism and acceptance
Some muhaddithun declared the hadith weak due to unknown narrators. The author defends it by noting the collective fame and the presence of Shu'bah in the chain.
Understanding why scholars accepted it is crucial for fatwa methodology.

" ومثل ماروى عن مَعْقِل المُزَنَى رضى الله تعالى عنه قال: "أَمَرَنِي النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ أنْ أَقْضِيَ بَيْنَ قَوْمٍ فَقُلْتُ: مَا أُحْسِنُ أَنْ أَقْضِيَ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ! قَالَ: اللَّهُ مَعَ الْقَاضِي مَا لَمْ يَحِف عَمْدا. " وكذلك بعث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعض الصحابة إلى البلاد النائية، فأذن لهم بالإفتاء والقضاء، كما أخرج المحدثون عن أصحاب مُعاذ بن جبل رضى الله تعالى عنه: "لَمَّا أَرَادَ أَنْ يَبْعَثُ مُعَاذَا إِلَى الْيَمَنِ، قَالَ: "كَيْفَ تَقْضِي إِذَا عَرَضَ لَكَ قَضَاءُ؟ " قَالَ: أَقْضِي بِكِتَابِ اللهِ . " قَالَ: "فَإِنْ لَمْ تَجِدْ فِي كِتَابِ اللهِ؟". قَالَ: "فَبِسُنَّةِ رَسُولِ اللهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: " قَالَ: "فَإِنْ لَمْ تَجِدْ فِى سُنَّةِ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ وَلا فِي كِتَابِ اللهِ؟ " قَالَ: "أَجْتَهِدُ رَأْيِى ولا ألو. " فَضَرَبَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ صَدْرَهُ فَقَالَ: "الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِي وَقَقَ رَسُولَ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ لِمَا يُرْضى رَسُولَ اللهِ " وهذا الحديث، وإن أعله بعض المحدثين بجهالة الحارث بن عمرو، وجهالة من روى عنهم من أصحاب معاذ بن جبل رضى الله عنه، ولكن تلقاه علماء كل عصر ومصر بالقبول، ويقول الحافظ ابن القيم رحمه الله تعالى: "فهذا حديث، وإن كان عن غير مُسمين فهم أصحاب معاذ، فلا يضره ذلك، لأنه يدل على شهرة الحديث، وأن الذي حدث به الحارث بن عمرو عن جماعة من أصحاب معاذ، لاواحدٍ منهم، وهذا أبلغ في الشهرة عن واحدٍ منهم لوسُمِّى. كيف وشهرةً أصحاب مُعاذ بالعلم والدين والفضل والصدق بالمحل الذى لا يخفى؟ ولا يُعرف في أصحابه متهم ولا كذاب ولا مجروح، بل أصحابه من أفاضل المسلمين وخيارهم، لا يشك أهل العلم بالنقل في ذلك. كيف وشعبة حامل لواء هذا الحديث؟ وقد قال بعض أئمة الحديث: إذا رأيت شُعبة في إسناد حديث فاشدد يديك به. قال أبوبكر الخطيب: وقد قيل إن عبادة بن نُسَي رواه عن عبد الرحمن بن غنم عن معاذ
The first to hold the position of ifta was the leader of the messengers and the seal of the prophets, peace be upon him. He used to issue fatwas from Allah by His clear revelation. His fatwas were comprehensive rulings and are the greatest source of Islamic law after the Noble Quran. The Companions memorized them in their hearts and in writings as established in the discussion on the recording and writing of hadith. No one in the era of the Messenger of Allah engaged in the position of ifta other than him, but he sometimes delegated the matter of ifta or qada to some of his Companions, perhaps to train them in ijtihad and deduction. For example, what al-Hakim narrated from Abdullah ibn Amr: 'Two men brought a dispute to the Prophet, so he said to Amr: Judge between them. Amr said: Should I judge between them while you are present, O Messenger of Allah? He said: Yes, with the understanding that if you are correct, you have ten rewards, and if you exert yourself and err, you have one reward.' And similar is what was narrated from Ma'qil al-Muzani: 'The Prophet ordered me to judge between a people, so I said: I do not know how to judge, O Messenger of Allah! He said: Allah is with the judge as long as he does not intentionally oppress.' Likewise, the Messenger of Allah sent some Companions to distant lands and permitted them to issue fatwas and judge. As the muhaddithun narrated from the Companions of Mu'adh ibn Jabal: 'When he wanted to send Mu'adh to Yemen, he said: How will you judge when a case is presented to you? He said: I will judge by the Book of Allah. He said: If you do not find in the Book of Allah? He said: Then by the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah. He said: If you do not find in the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah nor in the Book of Allah? He said: I will exert my opinion and not fall short. So the Messenger of Allah struck his chest and said: Praise be to Allah who has guided the messenger of the Messenger of Allah to what pleases the Messenger of Allah.' This hadith, although some muhaddithun declared it weak due to the unknown identity of al-Harith ibn Amr and the unknown people from the Companions of Mu'adh, yet the scholars of every age and region have accepted it. Hafiz Ibn al-Qayyim said: 'This hadith, even if from unnamed persons, they are the Companions of Mu'adh, so that does not harm it, because it indicates the fame of the hadith and that al-Harith ibn Amr narrated it from a group of Mu'adh's Companions, not just one; this is stronger in fame than if he had named one. How [can it be otherwise] while the fame of Mu'adh's Companions in knowledge, religion, virtue, and truthfulness is at a level that is not hidden? No one among his Companions is known to be accused, a liar, or discredited; rather, his Companions are among the best of Muslims and their elite, and people of knowledge in transmission do not doubt that. How [can it be] when Shu'bah is the carrier of the banner of this hadith? Some hadith imams have said: If you see Shu'bah in the chain of a hadith, hold fast to it. Abu Bakr al-Khatib said: It has been said that Ubadah ibn Nusay narrated it from Abd al-Rahman ibn Ghanm from Mu'adh.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents the historical origins of the office of ifta (issuing fatwas). The author, Mufti Muhammad Taqi Usmani, begins by establishing that the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ was the first and primary mufti, receiving revelation directly from Allah. His fatwas are the second source of Islamic law after the Quran. The Companions preserved these fatwas memorially and in writing. Notably, the Prophet did not allow others to hold the office of ifta during his lifetime, but he did delegate some Companions for qada (judging) and ifta as a training exercise. The author provides three examples: the hadith of 'Amr ibn al-'As, where the Prophet promised rewards for correct ijtihad and a single reward for incorrect ijtihad; the hadith of Ma'qil al-Muzani, where the Prophet assured divine assistance for a judge who does not oppress; and the famous hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal when sent to Yemen, where the Prophet approved Mu'adh's methodology of referring to Quran, Sunnah, and then ijtihad. This last hadith, despite some criticism regarding its chain, has been accepted by the scholarly community. The author defends its authenticity by citing the acceptance of scholars like Ibn al-Qayyim and the presence of Shu'bah in its chain. The passage provides the foundational basis for the permissibility and methodology of ijtihad by qualified scholars after the Prophet.

Key Terms
الإفتاء (al-ifta'): The office or act of issuing fatwas (legal rulings) based on revelation and ijtihad.
القضاء (al-qada'): Judging between disputing parties, a function delegated by the Prophet to some companions.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Exertion of mental effort by a qualified jurist to derive rulings from the sources when no clear text exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the Prophet's response when Mu'adh said he would exert his own opinion (ijtihad) if he didn't find a ruling in the Quran and Sunnah?
A) He criticized him for relying on personal opinion.
B) He struck his chest and praised Allah for guiding the messenger of His Messenger.
C) He told him to only follow the Quran and Sunnah.
D) He said ijtihad was only for the Prophet himself.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet established the foundational methodology for ifta and ijtihad, and this has been accepted by the Hanafi school as a core principle.

The Hierarchical Sources of Fatwa in the Salaf
Narrations from early Companions detailing the sequential sources for issuing fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of sources for fatwa as practiced by the Companions and identify the primary, secondary, tertiary, and final sources from their narrations.

Core Idea
The Quran first, then Sunnah, then consensus, then ijtihad — the Salaf's method is a clear hierarchy.

When the Companions were asked for a ruling, they did not improvise. They followed a disciplined sequence beginning with the Quran. This lesson examines four narrations that reveal this method, from Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Umar's letter. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for anyone who wishes to issue fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source
Locate where each Companion begins: the Quran. Ibn Mas'ud says 'look in the Book of Allah' first.
2
Identify the secondary source
Next, the Sunnah of the Prophet. All narrations agree on this step.
3
Identify the tertiary source
Ibn Mas'ud mentions 'what the Muslims have agreed upon' (consensus). Ibn Abbas mentions Abu Bakr and Umar. Zayd mentions 'people of opinion'. Consensus is the third step.
Note variation: consensus can be explicit or represented by leading Companions.
4
Identify the final source
After exhausting the above, they resort to personal ijtihad. Ibn Mas'ud says 'exert your opinion'; Zayd says 'exert your own effort'; Umar says 'measure matters by analogy'.
Ijtihad includes qiyas and preference (istihsan).
5
Recognize the consistent pattern
Despite small variations, the hierarchy is consistent: Quran → Sunnah → Consensus → Ijtihad. This pattern is the foundation of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخرج الدارمى أيضا عن عبدالله بن مسعود رضى الله عنه قال:" فإذا سئلتم عن شيئ فانظروا في كتاب الله، فإن لم تجدوه في كتاب الله ففى سنّةِ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فإن لم تجدوه في سنّةِ رسولِ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فما أجمع عليه المسلمون، فإن لم يكن فيما اجتمع عليه المسلمون فاجتهد رأيك."
وأخرج أيضا عن عبد الله بن يزيد قال: كان ابن عباس رضی الله تعالى عنهما إذا سُئل عن أمر ، فكان في القرآن أخبَرَ به، وإن لم يكن في القرآن وكان عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أخبر به. فإن لم يكن ، فعن أبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما، فإن لم يكن، قال فيه برأيه. (۱)
وأخرج البيهقى (۳) عن مَسْلَمَةَ بن مَخلَد أنّه قام على زيد بن ثابت رضى عنه فقال: يا ابن عمّ ! أَكْرهنا على القضاء، فقال زيد: "اقض بكتاب الله عز وجل، فإن لم يكن فى كتاب الله ففى سنّة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فإن لم يكن في سنّة النبى صلى الله عليه وسلم، فادع أهل الرأى ثم اجتهد، واختر لنفسك ولا حرج. " وكذلك أخرج البيهقي عن إدريس الأوْدِى عنه قال: "أخرج إلينا سعيد بن أبي بردة كتاباً، فقال: هذا كتاب عمر رضى الله إلى أبي موسى رضى الله عنه - فذكر الحديث وفيه - الفهم فيما يَخْتَلِجُ في صدرك مما لم يبلغك فى القرآن والسنة، فتعرّف الأمثال والأشباه ، ثمّ قِسِ الأمور عند ذلك واعمد إلى أحبها إلى الله وأشبهها فيما ترى .
فهؤلاء الصحابة رضى الله عنهم كلهم مشوا على الأصل الذي ذكر في حديث مُعاذ رضی الله عنه ممّا يُقوّى ذلك الحديث و يدل على صحة قول ابن القيم رحمه الله تعالى أن حديث مُعاذ عمل به السلف الصالحون.
(۱) سنن الدارمي، المقدمة، باب الفتيا وما فيه من الشدة، رقم ١٦٩ و ١٧١ و ١٦٨ بالترتيب المذكور
(۲) الإمام البيهقى: الإمام الحافظ العلامة الذى أيد المذهب الشافعي بتصانيفه، شيخ خراسان، أبو بكر أحمد الحسين بن علي بن موسى البيهقي ، نسبة إلى بيهق وهي قرى مجتمعة بنواحى بن نیسابور. ولد سنة ٣٨٤ هـ. . كان تلميذ الإمام الحاكم أبي عبد الله محمد عبد الله في بن الحديث. وألّف وصنّف كثيرا في الحديث والفقه وفنون شتى، وتواليفه تقارب ألف مما جزء يسبقه إليه أحد من أشهر تصانيفه "السنن الكبرى" و "شعب الإيمان" و "معرفة السنن والآثار".
ومن عجائب أخباره أنه صنف مثل هذه التصانيف مع أنه لم يكن عنده سنن النسائي ولا جامع الترمذي ولا سنن ابن ماجه كما ذكره الحافظ الذهبي رحمه الله سنة ٤٥٨ هـ الله. تُوفّي رحمه ملخص من تذكرة الحفاظ ۳ ۱۱۳۲ إلى ١١٣٥ والأنساب ١: ٤٣٨)
And al-Darimi also narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: 'When you are asked about something, look in the Book of Allah. If you do not find it in the Book of Allah, then in the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. If you do not find it in the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah, then in what the Muslims have agreed upon. If it is not in what the Muslims have agreed upon, then exert your opinion.' And he also narrated from Abdullah ibn Yazid who said: Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, when asked about a matter, if it was in the Quran, he would inform of it; if it was not in the Quran but was from the Messenger of Allah, he would inform of it; if not, then from Abu Bakr and Umar; if not, he would give his own opinion. (1) And al-Bayhaqi narrated from Maslamah ibn Mukhallad that he stood before Zayd ibn Thabit and said: O cousin! You have compelled us to judge. Zayd said: 'Judge by the Book of Allah, then by the Sunnah of the Prophet, then call the people of opinion, then exert your own effort, and choose for yourself without blame.' Likewise, al-Bayhaqi narrated from Idris al-Awdi from him who said: 'Sa'id ibn Abi Burda brought out a book to us and said: This is the book of Umar to Abu Musa, and he mentioned the hadith including: 'Understanding of what stirs in your heart regarding what has not reached you in the Quran and Sunnah. Then recognize similarities and analogies, then measure matters accordingly, and adopt the most beloved to Allah and most similar in your view.' These Companions, may Allah be pleased with them all, followed the principle mentioned in the hadith of Mu'adh, which strengthens that hadith and indicates the correctness of Ibn al-Qayyim's statement that the Salaf acted upon the hadith of Mu'adh. (1) Sunan al-Darimi, introduction, chapter on fatwa and its severity, numbers 169, 171, 168 in the order mentioned. (2) Al-Imam al-Bayhaqi: ...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the methodology of fatwa among the Salaf as preserved in narrations from the Companions. It opens with a narration from Ibn Mas'ud outlining a clear hierarchy: the Quran first, then the Sunnah, then consensus (ijma'), then ijtihad. This mirrors the famous hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal when he was sent to Yemen. The second narration shows the practice of Ibn Abbas, who also followed a similar order but included the opinions of Abu Bakr and Umar after the Sunnah and before his own opinion. The third narration from Zayd ibn Thabit adds the step of consulting people of opinion (ahl al-ra'y) before personal ijtihad, indicating that collective deliberation was encouraged. The fourth narration is the well-known letter of Umar to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, which explicitly instructs the use of analogy (qiyas) and preference for what is most beloved to Allah. The passage concludes by asserting that all these Companions acted upon the same principle found in the hadith of Mu'adh, confirming that the Salaf consistently followed this method. This is a foundational lesson for any mufti: the sources of law are hierarchical and ijtihad is only permissible after exhausting the primary sources.

Key Terms
السلف (al-salaf): The righteous predecessors, especially the Companions and early generations, whose methodology in fatwa is exemplary.
الفتوى (al-fatwa): A legal opinion issued by a qualified jurist (mufti) in response to a question, based on evidence from the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, or ijtihad.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Exertion of mental effort by a qualified scholar to derive a ruling when no explicit text exists, using tools like qiyas and istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration from Ibn Mas'ud, what is the third source to consult after the Quran and Sunnah?
A) Ijtihad
B) Consensus (ijma')
C) The opinions of Abu Bakr and Umar
D) Qiyas

Key Takeaway
The fatwa methodology of the Salaf is a fixed hierarchy: Quran, then Sunnah, then consensus, then ijtihad.

The Companions' Consensus on Fatwa Methodology
Narrations strengthening Mu'adh's hadith and showing the Salaf's consistent methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the narrations from Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Zayd, and Umar collectively confirm the principle of Mu'adh's hadith and evaluate Ibn al-Qayyim's claim that the Salaf acted upon it.

Core Idea
All four Companions followed the same methodology, confirming Mu'adh's hadith as the standard for fatwa.

The hadith of Mu'adh when he was sent to Yemen is well-known: he said he would judge by the Quran, then the Sunnah, then his own ijtihad. Some scholars questioned the chain or the practice. This lesson presents evidence from multiple Companions showing that they indeed followed that exact method, thereby strengthening the hadith's authenticity and applicability.

Guided Path
1
Recall the hadith of Mu'adh
Mu'adh's hadith: 'I will judge by the Book of Allah, then the Sunnah, then I will exert my own opinion.' This is the benchmark.
2
Compare each Companions' statement
Ibn Mas'ud adds consensus after Sunnah; Ibn Abbas includes Abu Bakr and Umar; Zayd includes people of opinion; Umar includes qiyas. All begin with Quran and Sunnah and end with personal reasoning.
Variations enrich but do not contradict the core.
3
Identify the common core
Every narration has: Quran first, then Sunnah second, then some form of collective or personal reasoning. This matches Mu'adh's sequence.
4
Understand the strengthening effect
Multiple independent narrations from different Companions reporting the same methodology corroborate the principle, even if the exact hadith of Mu'adh is debated in chain.
This is a form of mutaba'at (supporting narrations).
5
Appreciate Ibn al-Qayyim's claim
Ibn al-Qayyim stated that the Salaf acted upon Mu'adh's hadith. The passage says these narrations prove his correctness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأخرج أيضا عن عبد الله بن يزيد قال: كان ابن عباس رضی الله تعالى عنهما إذا سُئل عن أمر ، فكان في القرآن أخبَرَ به، وإن لم يكن في القرآن وكان عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أخبر به. فإن لم يكن ، فعن أبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما، فإن لم يكن، قال فيه برأيه. (۱)

ومن عجائب أخباره أنه صنف مثل هذه التصانيف مع أنه لم يكن عنده سنن النسائي ولا جامع الترمذي ولا سنن ابن ماجه كما ذكره الحافظ الذهبي رحمه الله سنة ٤٥٨ هـ الله. تُوفّي رحمه ملخص من تذكرة الحفاظ ۳ ۱۱۳۲ إلى ١١٣٥ والأنساب ١: ٤٣٨)
And al-Darimi also narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: 'When you are asked about something, look in the Book of Allah. If you do not find it in the Book of Allah, then in the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. If you do not find it in the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah, then in what the Muslims have agreed upon. If it is not in what the Muslims have agreed upon, then exert your opinion.' And he also narrated from Abdullah ibn Yazid who said: Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, when asked about a matter, if it was in the Quran, he would inform of it; if it was not in the Quran but was from the Messenger of Allah, he would inform of it; if not, then from Abu Bakr and Umar; if not, he would give his own opinion. (1) And al-Bayhaqi narrated from Maslamah ibn Mukhallad that he stood before Zayd ibn Thabit and said: O cousin! You have compelled us to judge. Zayd said: 'Judge by the Book of Allah, then by the Sunnah of the Prophet, then call the people of opinion, then exert your own effort, and choose for yourself without blame.' Likewise, al-Bayhaqi narrated from Idris al-Awdi from him who said: 'Sa'id ibn Abi Burda brought out a book to us and said: This is the book of Umar to Abu Musa, and he mentioned the hadith including: 'Understanding of what stirs in your heart regarding what has not reached you in the Quran and Sunnah. Then recognize similarities and analogies, then measure matters accordingly, and adopt the most beloved to Allah and most similar in your view.' These Companions, may Allah be pleased with them all, followed the principle mentioned in the hadith of Mu'adh, which strengthens that hadith and indicates the correctness of Ibn al-Qayyim's statement that the Salaf acted upon the hadith of Mu'adh. (1) Sunan al-Darimi, introduction, chapter on fatwa and its severity, numbers 169, 171, 168 in the order mentioned. (2) Al-Imam al-Bayhaqi: ...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the methodology of fatwa among the Salaf as preserved in narrations from the Companions. It opens with a narration from Ibn Mas'ud outlining a clear hierarchy: the Quran first, then the Sunnah, then consensus (ijma'), then ijtihad. This mirrors the famous hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal when he was sent to Yemen. The second narration shows the practice of Ibn Abbas, who also followed a similar order but included the opinions of Abu Bakr and Umar after the Sunnah and before his own opinion. The third narration from Zayd ibn Thabit adds the step of consulting people of opinion (ahl al-ra'y) before personal ijtihad, indicating that collective deliberation was encouraged. The fourth narration is the well-known letter of Umar to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, which explicitly instructs the use of analogy (qiyas) and preference for what is most beloved to Allah. The passage concludes by asserting that all these Companions acted upon the same principle found in the hadith of Mu'adh, confirming that the Salaf consistently followed this method. This is a foundational lesson for any mufti: the sources of law are hierarchical and ijtihad is only permissible after exhausting the primary sources.

Key Terms
السلف (al-salaf): The righteous predecessors, especially the Companions and early generations, whose methodology in fatwa is exemplary.
الفتوى (al-fatwa): A legal opinion issued by a qualified jurist (mufti) in response to a question, based on evidence from the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, or ijtihad.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Exertion of mental effort by a qualified scholar to derive a ruling when no explicit text exists, using tools like qiyas and istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the narrations from the Companions indicate about the hadith of Mu'adh?
A) They contradict it
B) They strengthen it
C) They are unrelated
D) They prove it is fabricated

Key Takeaway
The fatwa methodology of the Salaf is a fixed hierarchy: Quran, then Sunnah, then consensus, then ijtihad.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

Fatwa Categories Among the Companions: Mukthirun, Mutawassitun, Muqillun
Historical classification of Companions by fatwa quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of Companions based on fatwa output and explain why this classification matters for the weight of a transmitted opinion.

Core Idea
Only seven Companions are prolific fatwa-givers; the rest gave few rulings, affecting the authority of their transmitted opinions.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational piece of fatwa methodology: the classification of the Companions (Sahaba) according to how many fatwas they issued. This classification was developed by scholars like Ibn al-Qayyim and Ibn Hazm to help later jurists assess the reliability and weight of transmitted opinions. Understanding these categories is essential for any mufti to properly evaluate the authority of a Companion's ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Name the three groups: al-mukthirun (prolific), al-mutawassitun (intermediate), al-muqillun (few). Memorize the seven mukthirun: 'Umar, 'Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, 'Aisha, Zayd ibn Thabit, Ibn 'Abbas, Ibn 'Umar.
These seven are the primary sources of Companion fatwas.
2
Distinguish the mutawassitun
List the intermediate Companions (e.g., Abu Bakr, 'Uthman, Abu Hurayra). Note that their fatwa output is moderate, and each could fill a small volume.
This group includes many prominent figures but fewer transmitted rulings.
3
Understand the muqillun
Recognize that most Companions fall into this category, with only one or two rulings each. Their total fatwas would fill only a small volume.
Ibn al-Qayyim and Ibn Hazm disagreed on whether certain individuals like Ma'iz belonged here.
4
Apply the classification
Explain how a mufti would use this classification: a fatwa from a mukthirun Companion carries more weight than one from a muqillun, especially when there is conflict.
This helps in tarjih (preference) when multiple reports exist.

و يدل على صحة قول ابن القيم رحمه الله تعالى أن حديث مُعاذ عمل به السلف الصالحون. الفتوى في عهد الصحابة ذكر ابن القيم في إعلام الموقعين أن الذين حفظت منهم أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم مائة ونيف وثلاثون نفساً ما بين رجل وامرأة. فكان المُكثرون منهم سبعاً : عمر بن الخطاب، وعلي بن أبي طالب ، وعبد الله بن مسعود ، وعائشة أم المؤمنين ، وزيد بن ثابت ، وعبد الله بن عباس ، وعبد الله بن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنهم أجمعين . وذكر ابن حزم أنّه يُمكن أن يجمع فتاوى كلّ واحدٍ منهم سفر ضخيم قال : وقد جمع أبو بكر محمد بن موسى بن يعقوب ابن أمير المؤمنين المأمون فتيا عبد الله بن عبّاس رضي الله عنهما في عشرين كتاباً، وأبو بكر محمد المذكور أحد أئمّة الإسلام في العلم والحديث. و أما المتوسطون من الصحابة فيما رُوي عنهم. من الفتيا، فعددهم أكثر منهم : أبو بكر الصديق ، وأم سلمة ، وأنس بن مالك وأبو سعيد الخدري ، وأبو هريرة ، وعثمان بن عفان ، وعبد الله بن عمرو بن العاص ، وعبد الله بن الزبير ، وأبو موسى الأشعري ، وسعد بن أبي وقاص ، وسلمان الفارسي ، وجابر بن عبد الله ، ومُعاذ بن جبل ، وطلحة ، والزبير وعبد الرحمن بن عوف ، وعِمران بن حصين ، وأبو بكرة ، وعبادة بن الصامت ، ومُعاوية بن أبي سفيان رضي الله تعالى عنهم أجمعين . قال ابن حزم يمكن أن يُجمع من فتيا كل واحدٍ منهم جزء صغير جداً . والباقون من الصحابة رضي وسلم في ذلك هو فتوى لأنفسهما لجواز الإقرار، وقد أقرا عليها. فإن كان تخيّل هذا فما أبعده من خيال أو لعله ظفر عنهما بفتوى في شيء من الأحكام.
Methodologies of fatwa among the predecessors. He said: "Sa'id ibn Abi Burda brought out a book to us and said: This is the book of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) to Abu Musa (may Allah be pleased with him)." Then he mentioned the hadith, and in it: "Understanding regarding what occurs in your mind of what has not reached you in the Qur'an and Sunnah, then learn the similarities and parallels, then measure the matters accordingly, and aim for the most beloved to Allah and the most similar in what you see." So these Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, all followed the principle mentioned in the hadith of Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with him), which strengthens that hadith and indicates the correctness of Ibn al-Qayyim's statement that the righteous predecessors acted upon the hadith of Mu'adh. Fatwa in the era of the Companions: Ibn al-Qayyim mentioned in I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in that those from whom fatwa is preserved among the Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) are one hundred and thirty-odd persons, both men and women. Among them, the prolific (mukthirun) were seven: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, 'A'isha the Mother of the Believers, Zayd ibn Thabit, 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, and 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar - may Allah be pleased with them all. Ibn Hazm mentioned that it is possible to compile from the fatwa of each of them a huge volume. He said: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Musa ibn Ya'qub, son of the Commander of the Faithful al-Ma'mun, collected the fatwa of 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) in twenty books, and that Abu Bakr Muhammad is one of the leading scholars of Islam in knowledge and hadith. As for the intermediate (mutawassitun) among the Companions regarding what is transmitted from them of fatwa, their number is more than those: Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, Umm Salama, Anas ibn Malik, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, Abu Hurayra, 'Uthman ibn 'Affan, 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr ibn al-'As, 'Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Salman al-Farisi, Jabir ibn 'Abdullah, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Talha, al-Zubayr, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, 'Imran ibn Husayn, Abu Bakra, 'Ubada ibn al-Samit, and Mu'awiya ibn Abi Sufyan - may Allah be pleased with them all. Ibn Hazm said: From the fatwa of each of them a very small volume can be compiled. The remaining Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) are few in fatwas: from each of them only one or two issues are reported, and a slight increase on that, and from all of them together a small volume can be compiled after careful examination and research. Then Ibn al-Qayyim mentioned the names of these few (muqillun) from the Companions, quoting from Ibn Hazm (may Allah have mercy on them both). Then he expressed surprise that he counted among them Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyya (may Allah be pleased with them), and said: "Perhaps he imagined that their undertaking to permit confessing to zina without seeking permission from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in that was a fatwa from themselves for the permissibility of confession, and they were approved upon it. If he imagined this, how far-fetched is his imagination. Or perhaps he found a fatwa from them in some matter of rulings."
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage comes from a work on the principles and etiquette of issuing fatwa (Usul al-Ifta). It discusses the methodology of fatwa among the early generations (Salaf), particularly the Companions. The author begins by citing the well-known hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) approved his use of personal reasoning (ijtihad) when a ruling is not found in the Qur'an or Sunnah. This is supported by the letter of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) instructing Abu Musa al-Ash'ari to understand parallels and analogies. The ruling principle here is that qiyas (analogical reasoning) was practiced by the Companions. Then the author shifts to a historical overview of fatwa activity among the Companions, quoting Ibn al-Qayyim's I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in. He categorizes the Companions based on the volume of fatwas transmitted from them: the prolific (mukthirun) who gave many fatwas, the intermediate (mutawassitun) who gave fewer, and the few (muqillun) who gave very few. This categorization is important for understanding the hierarchy of scholarly authority. The author also mentions Ibn Hazm's observation that the fatwa of a single prolific Companion could fill a huge volume, while the fatwa of all the few together would fill only a small volume. The passage ends with Ibn al-Qayyim's critique of Ibn Hazm for including Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyya among the muqillun, questioning whether their actions constituted a fatwa.

Key Terms
المكثرون (al-mukthirūn): The prolific Companions from whom many fatwas are transmitted; specifically seven: Umar, Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Aisha, Zayd ibn Thabit, Ibn Abbas, and Ibn Umar.
المتوسطون (al-mutawassiṭūn): The intermediate Companions from whom a moderate number of fatwas are transmitted; a list of names is given.
المقلون (al-muqillūn): The few Companions from whom only one or two fatwas are transmitted; their total fatwas would fill only a small volume.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Approximately 100
C) Approximately 200
D) Approximately 50

Key Takeaway
The categorization of Companions into mukthirun, mutawassitun, and muqillun is a critical tool for evaluating the authority of transmitted fatwas.

Classification of Sahaba by Fatwa Frequency
Classification of Sahaba by fatwa frequency and mention of modern collections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three tiers of Sahaba fatwa frequency and explain why this classification is important for evaluating early legal authority.

Core Idea
Sahaba fatwa output varied widely; the mukthirun provide the richest juristic legacy.

This lesson introduces the historical classification of the Companions by the number of fatwas transmitted from them. Understanding this hierarchy helps the mufti assess the weight and reliability of different Sahaba in early Islamic law. The passage also critiques a methodological error and highlights modern efforts to collect these fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Note the Arabic terms: mukthirun (most prolific), mutawassitun (moderate), muqillun (few). Their names are given in the text.
The categories are based purely on the volume of narrated fatwas, not on the scholar's knowledge or status.
2
List the six mukthirun
These are: Umar, Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Aisha. Their fatwas were so many that they could fill volumes.
Ibn Abbas' fatwas alone filled twenty books, as compiled by Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Musa.
3
List the mutawassitun
Abu Bakr, Umm Salamah, Anas, Abu Sa'id, Abu Hurayrah, Uthman, and others (twenty in total). Ibn Hazm said their fatwas could each fill a very small portion.
These Companions were still highly knowledgeable but fewer rulings are preserved from them.
4
Understand the muqillun
Only one or two issues are narrated from each. Ibn al-Qayyim criticizes Ibn Hazm for including Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah, arguing their actions were not fatwas.
This shows the need for precise methodology: an action is not automatically a fatwa.
5
Recognize modern compilations
Contemporary scholars like Dr. Muhammad Rawwas Qal'aji and Shah Wali Allah have compiled encyclopedias of individual Companion fatwas.
These works help modern muftis access the early legal corpus systematically.

Methodologies of Fatwa among the Salaf
That it is possible to collect the fatwas of each one of them in a huge volume. He said: Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Musa ibn Ya'qub ibn Amir al-Mu'minin al-Ma'mun collected the fatwas of Abdullah ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) in twenty books, and the aforementioned Abu Bakr Muhammad is one of the imams of Islam in knowledge and hadith. As for the moderate ones among the Companions regarding what has been narrated from them of fatwas, their number is more than those: Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, Umm Salamah, Anas ibn Malik, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, Abu Hurayrah, Uthman ibn Affan, Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As, Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Salman al-Farsi, Jabir ibn Abdullah, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Talhah, al-Zubayr, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Imran ibn Husayn, Abu Bakrah, Ubadah ibn al-Samit, and Mu'awiyah ibn Abi Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with them all). Ibn Hazm said: It is possible to collect from the fatwa of each one of them a very small portion.
And the rest of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) are few in fatwas; from each of them only one or two issues are narrated, and a slight increase on that, and it is possible to collect from the fatwas of all of them a small portion after thorough investigation and research. Then Ibn al-Qayyim mentioned the names of these lesser Companions, quoting from Ibn Hazm (may Allah have mercy on them). Then he expressed surprise that he counted among them Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah (may Allah be pleased with them), and said: "Perhaps he imagined that their proceeding to permit confession of adultery without seeking permission from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in that matter was a fatwa from themselves to permit the confession, and they were affirmed upon it. If this is what he imagined, how far-fetched is such imagination? Or perhaps he came across a fatwa from them concerning some rulings."
(1) M
(1) I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in 1:28-29, Dar Ihya al-Turath al-Arabi, Beirut
