(1) Imam Wali Allah al-Dihlawi: He is Ahmad ibn Abd al-Rahim ibn Wajih al-Din al-Umari, al-Dihlawi al-Hanafi, the renewing imam. He was born (may Allah have mercy on him) in 1114 AH in the region of Muzaffarnagar, India. His father, the scholar Sheikh Abd al-Rahim, was among those who contributed to compiling Fatawa Hindiyyah at the request of the righteous Sultan Aurangzeb Alamgir (may Allah be exalted). He memorized the Quran at the age of seven, then devoted himself to seeking knowledge until he completed it at the age of fifteen, reading many basic books under his father. He engaged in teaching for twelve years. In 1143 AH he was honored to visit the two noble sanctuaries, residing there for two full years, keeping company with scholars in a noble companionship. He studied under Sheikh Abu Tahir Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Kurdi al-Madani and received from him the six mother books of hadith and others. Then he returned to the land of India where he revived hadith and its sciences after its lamp was almost extinguished there due to the predominance of interest in other sciences among its people. He has many works of extreme precision, among them: 'Hujjatullah al-Balighah', 'al-Fawz al-Kabir' on principles of tafsir, 'al-Musaffa Sharh al-Muwatta', commentary on the headings of al-Bukhari, 'al-Insaf' on the reasons for difference among mujtahids, and 'Aqd al-Jayyid fi Ahkam al-Ijtihad wa al-Taqlid'. He died (may Allah have mercy on him) in 1176 AH at the age of sixty-two. Summarized from Nuzhat al-Khawatir 6:398-415.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a historical survey of fatwa practices among the Companions of the Prophet ﷺ, classifying them by the volume of known fatwas. The author begins by noting that the six most prolific Companions—the 'mukthirun'—include Umar, Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Aisha. Their fatwas were so numerous that they could fill entire volumes, and indeed a collection of Ibn Abbas's fatwas alone filled twenty books. Next, the 'mutawassitun' (moderate ones) are listed; these include major figures like Abu Bakr, Uthman, Abu Hurayrah, and others, whose fatwas could each fill only a small portion. Finally, the 'muqillun' (less prolific) are those from whom only one or two rulings are transmitted. The passage then critiques Ibn Hazm's inclusion of Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah among the muqillun, suggesting that their act of confessing without permission was not a fatwa but a personal initiative. The section closes with a reference to modern compilations of Companion fatwas, highlighting the work of scholars like Muhammad Rawwas Qal'aji and the Hanafi renewer Shah Wali Allah al-Dihlawi, whose biographical note underscores the revival of hadith sciences in India. The overall purpose is to give the mufti a historical understanding of the sources and volume of early fatwa material.

Key Terms
مكثرون (mukthirūn): The Companions from whom the most fatwas are narrated; they are six: Umar, Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Aisha.
متوسطون (mutawassiṭūn): Companions with a moderate number of fatwas; the text lists twenty of them, including Abu Bakr, Umm Salamah, Abu Hurayrah, etc.
مقلون (muqillūn): Companions with very few fatwas; only one or two issues narrated from each.
مناهج الفتوى (manāhij al-fatwā): Methodologies and historical patterns of issuing legal opinions in early Islam.

Key Takeaway
The classification of Sahaba by fatwa frequency is essential for muftis to understand the historical development and weight of early legal opinions.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti

Two Early Fatwa Methodologies: Restrictive vs. Expansive
Comparing two early methodologies on issuing fatwa on hypothetical cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two early approaches to issuing fatwa on hypothetical cases, explain their evidence, and identify which methodology the Hanafi school follows.

Core Idea
Some early Salaf forbade fatwa on hypotheticals; others embraced it to build comprehensive fiqh.

Before the Hanafi school fully developed its expansive approach to fiqh, there was a fundamental debate among early scholars: should a mufti only answer questions about real events, or should he proactively derive rulings for hypothetical scenarios? This lesson presents both views with their textual proofs, setting the stage for understanding why the Hanafi tradition chose the second path.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the passage to distinguish the first group (hadith-focused, reluctant to use ra'y and qiyas) from the second group (dedicated to fiqh, compiled comprehensive books).
2
Understand the restrictive view
The first group held that a mufti should only answer questions about actual occurrences (baliyyah) that have happened, and should not engage in deriving rulings for hypotheticals.
They feared speculation would lead to division.
3
Examine the dalil for restriction
The passage quotes a marfu' mursal hadith: 'Do not be hasty with a calamity before it descends...' and an athar from Ibn Umar reporting Umar's curse on asking about what has not happened.
These are the primary texts cited by the restrictive group.
4
Consider the expansive view
The second group, exemplified by al-Sha'bi and Makhul, did not limit themselves to actual events but actively derived rulings and wrote books on all chapters of fiqh.
This approach became the foundation of systematic madhhab development.
5
Recognize the author's neutrality
The author presents both views without explicit preference, indicating that this is an established ikhtilaf among the Salaf.
The Hanafi school ultimately adopted the expansive methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: من كان معظم اشتغاله رواية الحديث، ولا يتكلم في الفقه إلا بما كان صريحًا فى الكتاب والسنّة، ولم يكن يصرف همه إلى استنباط المسائل الجزئية التي لم تقع بعد، وكان ذلك من أجل أن مُعْظَمهم كانوا يكرهون الخوض في الرأي و القياس، ويهابون الفتيا والاستنباط إلا لضرورة لا يجدون منها بداً. واستندوا فى ذلك بما روى عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه عاب كثرة السؤال.
والقسم الثاني: مَن نَصَبَ نفسه للفقه والفتوى، فلم يقتصر على رواية الأحاديث والآثار، بل اجتهد في جمع المسائل وتفريع الجزئيات، حتى كان له في كل باب من الفقه فتوى. ومنهم من دون فقهه في كتاب، مثل الشعبي ومكحول)
الفقهاء الذين منعوا من الإفتاء فيما لم يقع
أما القسم الأول، فقد ذهب إلى أنّ المفتى أو الفقية ينبغي أن يقتصر على المسائل الواقعة الحقيقية التى عرضت عليه من أحدٍ ممن ابتلى بتلك المسائل، ولا ينبغي للفقيه أن يشتغل بتفريع الجزئيات و بيانِ حُكم الأمور التي لم تقع فعلاً، فإنّه غير مكلف بذلك. وربما يُستدل على صحة هذا القول بالحديث المرفوع المروى عن أبي سلمة بن عبد الرحمن مرسلا، قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "لاتَسْتَعْجِلُوا  ِبالبَلِيَّةِ قَبْلَ نُزُولِهَا، فَإِنَّكُمْ إِذَا فَعَلْتُمْ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَزَلْ مِنْكُمْ مَنْ يُوَفَّقٌ وَيُسَدَّدٌ، وَإِنَّكُمْ إِنِ اسْتَعْجَلْتُمْ بِهَا قَبْلَ نُزُولِهَا تَفَرَّقَتْ بِكُمُ السُّبُلُ هَهُنَا وَهَهُنَا. وَأَشَارَ عَنْ يَمِيْنِهِ وَعَنْ شِمَالِهِ." أخرجه البيهقى فى المدخل وأخرجه الدارمي في مقدمة سننه عن وهب بن عمرو الجُمحي قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "لأَتَعْجَلُوا بِالْبَلِيَّةِ قَبْلَ نُزُولُهَا، فَإِنَّكُمْ إِنْ لا تَعْجَلُوْهَا قَبْلَ نُزُولِهَا لَا يَنْفَكَ الْمُسْلِمُوْنَ وَفِيْهِمْ، إِذَا هِيَ نَزَلَت مَنْ إِذَا قَالَ وُفِّقَ وَسُدَّدَ، وَإِنَّكُمْ إِنْ تَعْجَلُوْهَا، تَخْتَلِف بِكُمُ الْأَهْوَاءُ فَتَأْخُذُوا هَكَذَا وَهَكَذَا " وَأَشَارَبَيْنَ يَدَيْهِ وَعَنْ يَمِيْنِهِ وَعَنْ شِمَالِهِ. وأخرج الدارمى عن عدةٍ من الصحابة والتابعين كراهية الإفتاء فيما لم يقع فأخرج عن حماد بن زيد المِنْقَرِي، قال حدثني أبي قال : "جاء رجل يوما إلى ابن عمر فسأله عن شيئ لا أدري ما هو ، فقال له ابن عمر لا تسأل عما لم يكن، فإنّي سمعت عمر بن الخطاب يلعن من سأل عمّا لم يكن."
The first group: Those whose main occupation was narration of hadith, and they would not speak in fiqh except what was clear in the Book and Sunnah, and they did not devote their attention to deriving subsidiary issues that had not yet occurred. This was because most of them disliked delving into opinion (ra'y) and analogy (qiyas), and they feared giving fatwa and derivation except out of necessity from which they found no escape. They relied for this on what was narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he criticized excessive questioning. The second group: Those who dedicated themselves to fiqh and fatwa, not restricting themselves to narrating hadith and athar, but rather exerted effort in collecting issues and branching out subsidiary details, until they had a fatwa in every chapter of fiqh. Among them were those who wrote down their fiqh in books, such as al-Sha'bi and Makhul. The jurists who forbade giving fatwa on what has not occurred. As for the first group, they held that the mufti or faqih should restrict himself to real occurring cases that were presented to him by someone who was afflicted by those cases, and it is not proper for the faqih to occupy himself with branching out subsidiary details and explaining the rulings of matters that have not actually occurred, for he is not obligated to do so. Perhaps the correctness of this view is supported by the marfu' hadith narrated by Abu Salama ibn Abd al-Rahman in mursal form: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not be hasty with a calamity before it descends. If you do that, there will always be among you those who will be guided and set right. But if you are hasty with it before its descent, the ways will scatter from you here and here.' And he pointed to his right and left. Al-Bayhaqi recorded it in al-Madkhal, and al-Darimi recorded it in the introduction to his Sunan from Wahb ibn Amr al-Jumahi who said the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not be hasty with a calamity before its descent, for if you are not hasty with it before its descent, the Muslims will not cease to have among them, when it descends, someone who when he speaks will be guided and set right. But if you are hasty with it, desires will differ among you, and you will take this way and that way.' And he pointed in front of him, to his right, and to his left. Al-Darimi also recorded from several companions and followers the dislike of giving fatwa on what has not occurred. He narrated from Hammad ibn Zaid al-Minqari, who said my father narrated to me: 'One day a man came to Ibn Umar and asked him about something I do not know what it was. Ibn Umar said to him: Do not ask about what has not yet happened, for I heard Umar ibn al-Khattab cursing the one who asks about what has not happened.'
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage describes two distinct methodologies of fatwa among the early Salaf. The first group, predominantly hadith narrators, avoided giving rulings on hypothetical scenarios unless absolutely necessary, relying on a hadith that criticizes hasty inquiry into calamities before they occur. They feared that speculation would lead to division. The second group, exemplified by jurists like al-Sha'bi and Makhul, actively engaged in deriving rulings for potential cases, compiling comprehensive legal works. The text then quotes a marfu' mursal hadith through Abu Salama discouraging haste in dealing with afflictions (baliyyah), and a narration from Ibn Umar who, echoing his father Umar, forbade asking about hypotheticals. These serve as proof for those who restrict fatwa to actual occurrences. The author presents both views without explicit preference here, setting the stage for further discussion on the proper scope of ijtihad and fatwa.

Key Terms
بليَّة (baliyyah): A calamity or affliction; here specifically a legal problem or event that tests the jurist.
الإفتاء فيما لم يقع (al-ifta' fima lam yaqa'): Issuing a legal ruling on a matter that has not actually occurred yet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'Give me time to research.'
B) 'Do not ask about what has not yet happened, for I heard Umar ibn al-Khattab cursing the one who asks about what has not happened.'
C) 'This is a valid question; let me derive an answer.'
D) 'Go ask someone else.'

Key Takeaway
The early Salaf differed on whether to issue fatwa on hypothetical cases, with evidence from hadith and companion practice supporting both sides; the Hanafi school follows the expansive approach.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
beginner

The Salaf's Prohibition of Fatwa on Hypotheticals
Narrations forbidding fatwa on unoccurred events

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Salaf's principle that a mufti should not issue a fatwa on an event that has not yet occurred, and apply this principle to assess a mufti's conduct in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Do not hasten to answer what has not happened; wait until the real question arises.

This lesson examines a collection of prophetic and companion-era narrations that establish a fundamental adab (etiquette) for the mufti: refraining from issuing legal opinions about hypothetical situations that have not yet occurred. The Prophet (ﷺ) warned against rushing into answering such 'trials' before they descend, and the Companions consistently refused to engage with questions about unactualized events. Understanding this principle is crucial for maintaining humility and preventing unnecessary legal speculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prophetic command
Read the hadith reported by Abu Salama: 'Do not hasten towards the trial before its descent.' Note that 'trial' (balyah) here refers to a novel legal question or calamity. The Prophet prohibits premature answering.
The hadith is mursal (from a tabi'i directly attributing to Prophet) but accepted in adab context.
2
Examine companion practices
Look at the narrations from Umar, Ibn Umar, Zaid ibn Thabit, Ammar, and Ubayy. They consistently asked 'Akan ba'd?' (Has it happened?). If not, they refused to answer, sometimes even cursing the questioner.
This shows a strong repulsion to hypotheticals.
3
Understand the rationale
The Prophet warned that premature speculation leads to division and misguidance. The Salaf wanted to base fatwa on actual situations to avoid unnecessary disputes and to rely on divine guidance as events unfold.
This is not a blanket ban on all hypothetical reasoning (like qiyas), but on issuing fatwa for events not yet faced by the questioner.
4
Apply to mufti conduct
A modern mufti should similarly refrain from issuing definitive rulings on scenarios that are purely speculative and not actually occurring. Instead, he should wait until the issue arises or the questioner is truly in need.
This adab preserves the mufti's humility and accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وعن الزهري قال : "بلَغَنا أنّ زيد بن ثابت الأنصاري كان يقول إذا سُئل عن الأمر: أكان هذا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، قد كان حدث فيه بالذى يعلم والذي ،يرى، وإن قالوا: لم يكن، قال: فذروه حتى يكون "

وأخرج عن الشعبي، عن مسروق ، قال : سألت أبي بن كعب عن شيء، فقال: "أكان بعد؟ " قلت : لا ، قال : فأجمنا حتى يكون ، فإذا كان اجتهدنا لك رأينا " وأخرج عن موسى بن عليّ ، أنه سأل ابن شهاب عن شيء ، فقال ابن شهاب : "ما سمعت فيه بشيء، وما نزل بنا". فقلت : "إنه قد نزل ببعض إخوانك ، فقال : "ما سمعت فيه بشيئ وما نزل بنا، وما أنا بقائل فيه شيئاً. "
The methodologies of fatwa among the Salaf: (1) The marfu' narration from Abu Salama ibn Abd al-Rahman as a mursal: the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not hasten towards the trial before its descent, for if you do that, there will always be among you one who is guided and directed. But if you hasten towards it before its descent, the paths will scatter you here and there.' And he pointed to his right and left. Al-Bayhaqi recorded it in al-Madkhal; and al-Darimi recorded in the introduction to his Sunan from Wahb ibn Amr al-Jumahi that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not hasten towards the trial before its descent. For if you do not hasten towards it before its descent, the Muslims will not cease to have among them, when it descends, one who when he speaks is guided and directed. But if you hasten towards it, desires will differ among you, and you will take this way and that way.' And he pointed between his hands, to his right and left. And al-Darimi recorded from a number of companions and followers the dislike of giving fatwa about what has not occurred. He narrated from Hammad ibn Zaid al-Minqari, who said: my father narrated to me: 'A man came to Ibn Umar and asked him about something I do not know what it was. Ibn Umar said to him: Do not ask about what has not happened, for I heard Umar ibn al-Khattab curse the one who asks about what has not happened.' And from al-Zuhri: 'It has reached us that Zaid ibn Thabit al-Ansari used to say, when asked about a matter: Did this happen? If they said: Yes, it happened, he would speak about it with what he knew and what he thought. If they said: No, it did not happen, he would say: Leave it until it happens.' And from Amir: 'Ammar ibn Yasir was asked about a matter, and he said: Did this happen after? They said: No. He said: Leave us until it happens; when it happens, we will undertake it for you.' And from Tawus: 'Umar said on the pulpit: I make it binding by Allah upon a man who asks about what has not happened, for Allah has made clear what is to come.' And al-Khatib narrated from Ibn Umar: 'O people, do not ask about what has not happened, for Umar used to curse or revile the one who asked about what has not happened.' And from al-Sha'bi, from Masruq: 'I asked Ubayy ibn Ka'b about something, and he said: Did it happen after? I said: No. He said: Then let us rest until it happens; when it happens, we will exert our opinion for you.' And from Musa ibn Ali: 'He asked Ibn Shihab about something, and Ibn Shihab said: I have not heard anything about it, and it has not befallen us. I said: It has befallen some of your brothers. He said: I have not heard anything about it, and it has not befallen us, and I will not say anything about it.'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a series of narrations from the Prophet (ﷺ), the Companions, and the early Followers (Salaf) establishing a clear methodological rule: a mufti should not issue a fatwa regarding an event that has not yet occurred. The Prophet (ﷺ) explicitly forbade hastening towards the 'trial' (balyah) before it descends, warning that premature speculation leads to division and misguidance. The Companions like Umar ibn al-Khattab, Ibn Umar, Zaid ibn Thabit, Ammar ibn Yasir, and Ubayy ibn Ka'b consistently refused to answer hypothetical questions, often asking first whether the event had actually happened. Their practice was to wait until the event occurred and then exert their best judgment. This principle is grounded in the concern that answering hypotheticals leads to unnecessary theological and legal disputes, distracts from actual problems, and may cause people to act on unverified opinions. The mufti is therefore advised to restrict himself to answering questions about real situations that people face, as emphasized by Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri's refusal to opine on a matter that had not befallen him. This is a core adab (etiquette) for the mufti: humility and restraint in the face of the unknown.

Key Terms
بَلِيَّة (balyah): A trial or calamity; in this context, a novel legal issue or question that the mufti is asked about.
الْفَتَوَى (al-fatwa): A formal legal opinion issued by a qualified mufti in response to a question.
الإجمام (al-ijmam): Resting or refraining; used in the narration of Masruq where Ubayy says 'faa-jimna' meaning 'let us rest/refrain until it happens'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the general practice of the Salaf when asked about a matter that had not yet occurred?
A) They would answer immediately to provide guidance.
B) They would refuse to answer and wait until it occurred.
C) They would refer the question to a higher authority.
D) They would answer based on analogy (qiyas).

Key Takeaway
A mufti must not issue a fatwa on a hypothetical event that has not occurred; he should wait until the real question arises.

The Salaf's Approaches to Hypothetical Questions: Caution vs. Education
Presents two Salaf positions on answering hypothetical fiqh questions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Salaf positions on answering hypothetical fiqh questions and explain the Hanafi rationale for permitting such discussions for educational purposes.

Core Idea
The Salaf avoided hypotheticals for fatwa, but later scholars permitted them for training mujtahids.

This passage presents a key adab issue for muftis: should one answer questions about events that have not occurred? The Salaf varied, with some outright refusing, while later Hanafi scholars like al-Bayhaqi allowed such discussions for educational purposes. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti's approach to hypotheticals in classroom vs. fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Distinguish between the first group of Salaf (Companions and Followers) who refused to answer hypothetical questions, and the second group (later jurists like al-Halimi and al-Bayhaqi) who permitted them for teaching ijtihad.
The first group includes Zayd ibn Thabit, Ammar ibn Yasir, Umar, Ibn Umar, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, and Ibn Shihab.
2
Analyze the narrations of the first group
Examine each narration: they consistently asked whether the event had occurred. If not, they postponed the answer. This was based on the Prophetic hadith discouraging excessive questioning.
3
Understand the reinterpretation by later scholars
Al-Bayhaqi and al-Khatib interpreted the Salaf's avoidance not as a prohibition but as personal scrupulousness (tawru'). They argued that discussing hypotheticals is permissible for guiding students (mutafaqqiha) in the method of ijtihad.
Al-Bayhaqi quotes al-Halimi who explicitly permits it for teaching purposes, not for immediate action.
4
Apply the distinction to mufti practice
Recognize that the Hanafi school distinguishes between giving a fatwa for a real incident (where hypotheticals are avoided) and academic instruction (where hypotheticals are encouraged).
The mufti should avoid answering purely hypothetical questions in a fatwa context but may discuss them in a learning setting.
5
Note the Hanafi position and its evidence
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that hypothetical questions are permissible for educational purposes, based on the need to prepare future jurists and the reinterpretation of the Salaf's practice as caution, not prohibition.
Al-Khatib also explains that the Prophet's prohibition was out of mercy to reduce burden, not to block ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن الزهري قال : "بلَغَنا أنّ زيد بن ثابت الأنصاري كان يقول إذا سُئل عن الأمر: أكان هذا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، قد كان حدث فيه بالذى يعلم والذي يرى، وإن قالوا: لم يكن، قال: فذروه حتى يكون" وعن عامر قال : سُئِل عمّار بن ياسر عن مسألة، فقال: هل كان هذا بعد؟ قالوا: لا، قال: دعونا حتى تكون، فإذا كانت تجَسَّمْنَاها لكم . (۳) ۱۹ وعن طاوس (1) قال : قال عمر على المنبر: أحَرِّج بالله على رجل سأل عما لم يكن، فإن الله قد بين ما هو كائن. وأخرج الخطيب عن ابن عمر ، قال : "يا أيها الناس لا تسألوا عما لم يكن، فإن عمر كان يلعن ، أو يسب من سأل عما لم يكن." وأخرج عن الشعبي، عن مسروق ، قال : سألت أبي بن كعب عن شيء، فقال: "أكان بعد؟" قلت : لا ، قال : فأجمنا حتى يكون ، فإذا كان اجتهدنا لك رأينا" وأخرج عن موسى بن عليّ ، أنه سأل ابن شهاب عن شيء ، فقال ابن شهاب : "ما سمعت فيه بشيء، وما نزل بنا". فقلت : "إنه قد نزل ببعض إخوانك"، فقال : "ما سمعت فيه بشيئ وما نزل بنا، وما أنا بقائل فيه شيئاً." وأخرج عن مالك ، قال : " أدركت هذه البلدة وإنهم ليكرهون هذا الإكثار الذي فيه اليوم، يريد المسائل" الفقهاء الذين أفتوا فى المسائل التى لم تقع، وحجتهم أما القسم الثانى من العلماء هم الذين نَصَبوا أنفسهم لبيان الأحكام الفقهية وتدوينها بما يُيَسِّرُ لمن جاء بعدهم الوقوف عليها عند الحاجة، فتكلموا في المسائل المحتملة التي لم تقع بعد وحمَلُوا ماذكرنا من آثار الصحابة والتابعين على التورّع والاحتياط. فقال البيهقى رحمه الله تعالى بعد نقل هذه الآثار: وبلغنى عن أبي عبد الله الحليمي رحمه الله أنه أباح ذلك للمتفقهة الذين غرض العالم من جوابهم تنبيههم وإرشادهم إلى طريق النظر والإرشاد، لا ليعملوا " ثمّ قال الإمام البيهقى: " وعلى هذا الوجهِ وضعَ الفقهاء مسائل المجتهدات، وأجروا بآراءهم فيها لما في ذلك من إرشاد المتفقهة، وتنبيههم على كيفية الاجتهاد. وقال الخطيب بعد رواية آثار الصحابة والتابعين: " فهذا ما تعلّق به مَن مَنَع من الكلام فى الحوادثِ قبل نزولها ، ونحن نجيب عنه بمشيئة الله وعونه : أما كراهة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم المسائل، فإنما كان ذلك إشفاقاً على أمته ورأفة بها ، وتحتناً عليها"
From al-Zuhri: 'It reached us that Zayd ibn Thabit al-Ansari used to say when asked about a matter: Did this happen? If they said: Yes, it happened, he would speak about it with what he knew and what he saw. If they said: No, he said: Leave it until it happens.' From Amir: 'Ammar ibn Yasir was asked about a matter, and he said: Did this happen after? They said: No. He said: Leave us until it happens; when it happens we will exert for you.' From Tawus: 'Umar said on the pulpit: I make it binding by Allah upon any man who asks about what has not happened, for Allah has made clear what is to happen.' Al-Khatib narrated from Ibn Umar: 'O people, do not ask about what has not happened, for Umar used to curse or revile one who asked about what has not happened.' From al-Sha'bi from Masruq: 'I asked Ubayy ibn Ka'b about something, and he said: Did it happen after? I said: No. He said: Then let us rest until it happens; when it happens we will exert our opinion for you.' From Musa ibn Ali: 'He asked Ibn Shihab about something, and Ibn Shihab said: I have not heard anything about it, and it has not befallen us. I said: It has befallen some of your brothers. He said: I have not heard anything about it, and it has not befallen us, and I am not one to say anything about it.' From Malik: 'I found this town and they dislike this abundance that is in it today, meaning the questions.' The jurists who gave fatwas on questions that have not occurred—their evidence. As for the second group of scholars, they are those who set themselves to explain legal rulings and write them down to make it easy for those after them to find them when needed, so they spoke about potential questions that have not yet occurred and explained the narrations we mentioned from the Companions and Followers as scrupulousness and caution. Al-Bayhaqi said after quoting these narrations: 'It reached me that Abu Abd Allah al-Halimi permitted that for the students of fiqh whose purpose from the answer is to alert them and guide them to the path of consideration and guidance, not to act upon it.' Then Imam al-Bayhaqi said: 'And in this manner the jurists placed the questions of ijtihad and exercised their opinions in them because of what is in that of guiding the students and alerting them to the method of ijtihad.' Al-Khatib said after narrating the narrations of the Companions and Followers: 'This is what those who prohibit speaking about events before their occurrence cling to. And we answer it, with Allah's will and help: As for the dislike of the Messenger of Allah of questions, that was out of compassion for his ummah and mercy upon them, and as a reduction upon them.'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents two contrasting methodologies among the Salaf regarding hypothetical legal questions (masa'il lam taqa'). The first group, represented by Companions like Zayd ibn Thabit, Ammar ibn Yasir, Umar, Ibn Umar, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, and Ibn Shihab, refrained from answering questions about events that had not yet occurred. They would ask first whether the situation had actually taken place; if not, they would postpone the answer. This was based on the Prophetic sunnah to avoid unnecessary questioning, as seen in the hadith 'Do not ask me about what I have not spoken of.' The second group, including scholars like al-Halimi and al-Bayhaqi, allowed discussing hypotheticals for educational purposes—to train students (mutafaqqiha) in the method of ijtihad and analogy (qiyas). They interpreted the earlier Salaf's avoidance as personal scrupulousness (wara') and caution, not a prohibition. Al-Bayhaqi explicitly states that jurists wrote books on hypothetical matters (masa'il al-mujtahidat) precisely to guide future generations. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi further explains that the Prophet's prohibition was out of mercy to reduce burden, not to block the door of ijtihad. Hence, the Hanafi fatwa methodology permits discussing hypotheticals when the aim is scholarly training and preparation for future events.

Key Terms
المسائل التي لم تقع (al-masā’il allatī lam taqa‘): Hypothetical legal questions about events that have not yet occurred in reality.
التورّع والاحتياط (al-tawru‘ wa al-iḥtiyāṭ): Scrupulousness and caution; the justification given by later scholars for the Salaf's avoidance of hypotheticals.
المتفقهة (al-mutafaqqiha): Students of fiqh who are learning the method of ijtihad and derivation of rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to al-Bayhaqi, what was the purpose of answering hypothetical questions?
A) To act upon them immediately
B) To guide and train students in ijtihad
C) To create a body of binding law
D) To test the questioner's knowledge

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits discussing hypothetical questions for educational purposes, distinguishing between fatwa for real events and academic training of future jurists.

The Permissibility of Answering Hypothetical Questions
Debate on hypothetical questions and reconciliation of conflicting reports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa on a hypothetical scenario by applying the Hanafi ruling that hypothetical questions are permissible unless asked with obstinacy.

Core Idea
Hypothetical questions are permissible for fatwa; only obstinate questioning is prohibited.

This lesson examines a classical debate about whether a mufti may answer questions about events that have not yet occurred. The Hanafi school, based on the practice of the Companions and consensus, upholds permissibility, reconciling apparent prohibitions from some early scholars. You will learn to distinguish between genuine inquiry and obstinate questioning (ta'annut), and apply this distinction in fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The core issue is whether a mufti may answer questions about hypothetical situations (ma lam yakun). Recognize that the question itself is about permissibility of discussing rulings before their occurrence.
2
Distinguish between genuine inquiry and ta'annut
Examine the text: 'Umar punished Subaygh because he asked with ta'annut (obstinacy) to cause doubt. Contrast this with the discussions of 'Ali and the Tabi'un on fara'id, which were genuine. This distinction is key to reconciling the conflicting reports.
The hadith about 'ughlutat' applies to tricky questions meant to confuse, not genuine hypotheticals.
3
Analyze the conflicting reports
Some early scholars (Zayd, Ubayy, Tawus, Mu'adh) refrained from answering hypotheticals due to fear of error. Others (Umar, Ali, later jurists) engaged in them. Understand that the author interprets the former as personal piety, not a general prohibition, and the latter as the established practice leading to consensus.
4
Apply the consensus rule
Al-Khatib reports that the practice of the Companions and Tabi'un, and the consensus of later jurists, establish that answering hypotheticals is permissible. In Hanafi usul, consensus (ijma) is a binding proof, overriding individual caution.
The consensus is based on their discussions of fara'id and other rulings before actual cases.
5
Formulate a fatwa
When a questioner approaches with a hypothetical, the mufti must first assess intent. If the intent is genuine learning or preparation, answer. If obstinate (ta'annut), decline. This balances permissibility with adab al-mufti.
The mufti's role is to guide, not to entertain argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كثيرة لما ذكرناه وأما تحريج عمر فى السؤال عما لم يكن ولعنه من فَعَل ذلك، فيحتمل أن يكون قصد به السؤال على سبيل التعنّت والمغالطة ، لا على سبيل التفقه وابتغاء الفائدة . ولهذا ضربَ صَبيغ بن عسل ونفاه، وحرمه رزقه وعطاءه ، لمّا سأل عن حروف من مشكل القرآن ، فخَشِي عمر أن يكون قصد بمسألته ضعفاء المسلمين فى العلم ، ليُوقع في قلوبهم التشكيك والتضليل بتحريف القرآن عن نهج التنزيل ، وصرفه عن صواب القول فيه إلى فاسد التأويل ، ومثل هذا قد ورد عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم النهي عنه والذم لفاعله. " ثم أخرج الخطيب حديث معاوية رضى الله عنه أن النبيَّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ نَهَى عَنِ الأُغْلُوْطَات. قال عيسى : والأغلوطات : ما لا يحتاج إليه من كيف وكيف وحديث ثوبان رضی الله تعالى عنه عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، قال : "سَيَكُونُ أَقْوَامٌ مِنْ أُمَّتِى يُغَلِّطُوْنَ فُقَهَاءَهُمْ بِعَضْلُ الْمَسَائِل، أُولَئِكَ شِرَارُ أُمَّتِى." وأخرج عن الحسن البصري رحمه الله تعالى قال: الثيرار عباد الله ينتقون شرار المسائل يُعْمُون بها عباد الله." ثم قال الخطيب رحمه الله تعالى: "وقد روى عن عمر بن الخطاب ، وعلي بن أبي طالب ، وغيرهما من الصحابة أنهم تكلّموا في أحكام الحوادث قبل نزولها ، وتناظروا في علم الفرائض والمواريث ، وتبعهم على هذه السبيل التابعون ، ومن بعدهم من فقهاء الأمصار ، فكان ذلك إجماعاً منهم على أنه جائز غيرُ مكروه، ومباح غير محظور. وأما حديث زيد بن ثابت ، وأبي بن كعب(۱) صبيغ بن عسل كان رجلاً يسأل الناس عن أشياء من مشكل القرآن ومتشابهه، وقد روى ابن عساكر فيه روايات مختلفة كلّها إلى أن الله تعالى عنه ضربه ونهى المسلمين عن عمر رضی ترجع مجالسته لوقوعه في الأغاليط والمتشابهات. راجع له تاريخ دمشق لابن عساكر ٢٣: ٤٠٨ إلى ٤١٣
وعمار بن ياسر رضى الله تعالى عنهم ، فإنّه محمول على أنهم توقَّوُوا القول برأيهم خوفاً من الزلل، وهيبة لما فى الاجتهاد من الخطر ، ورأوا أن لهم عن ذلك مندوحة فيما لم يحدث من النوازل ، وأن كلامهم فيها إذا حدثت تدعو إليه الحاجة فيوفِّق الله فى تلك الحالِ مَن قصد إصابة الحق. وقد روى عن معاذ بن جبل نحو هذا القول. " ثم أخرج بسنده عن الصلت بن راشد قال : سألت طاوساً عن شيئ ، فانتهرنى ، وقال: أكان هذا ؟ قلت : نعم! وقال : الله؟ ، قلت : الله ! ، قال : إن أصحابنا أخبرونا عن معاذ بن جبل أنه قال : " أيُّها الناس! لا تعجلوا بالبلاء قبل نزوله، فيذهب بكم ها هنا وها هنا ، فإنّكم إن لم تعجلوا بالبلاء قبل نزوله ، لم ينفك المسلمون أن يكون فيهم من إذا سئل سدد ، أو قال: وفّق . " قال الخطيب: "وهذا فعل أهل الورع والمُشْفِقين على دينهم. "
Many [reasons] for what we mentioned. As for 'Umar's rebuking of asking about what has not occurred and his cursing of the one who did that, it is possible that he intended by the question the way of obstinacy and fallacious argument, not the way of seeking understanding and seeking benefit. For this reason, he beat Subaygh ibn 'Asl, exiled him, and deprived him of his sustenance and stipend, because he asked about letters from the problematic parts of the Qur'an. 'Umar feared that by his question he intended the weak among the Muslims in knowledge, to cause doubt and misguidance in their hearts by distorting the Qur'an from the path of revelation, and diverting it from correct speech about it to corrupt interpretation. Similar to this has been reported from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in prohibition and condemnation of its doer. Then al-Khatib narrated the hadith of Mu'awiyah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited 'al-ughlutat'. 'Isa said: 'Al-ughlutat' are what is not needed of 'how and how'. And the hadith of Thawban from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'There will be people from my nation who will confuse their scholars with difficult questions; those are the worst of my nation.' And he narrated from al-Hasan al-Basri who said: 'The pious servants of God choose the worst questions to blind the servants of God.' Then al-Khatib said: 'It has been reported from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, and other Companions that they spoke about rulings of events before their occurrence, and deliberated in the science of fara'id and inheritances; the Tabi'un followed them on this path, and after them the jurists of the regions. That was consensus from them that it is permissible, not disliked, and allowed, not prohibited. As for the hadith of Zayd ibn Thabit, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, and 'Ammar ibn Yasir, it is interpreted that they refrained from speaking with their own opinion out of fear of slipping, and awe of the danger in ijtihad, and they saw that they had leeway in that regarding what had not occurred of incidents, and that their speech about them when they occur necessitates need, and God grants success in that situation to whomever intends to reach the truth. And similar has been reported from Mu'adh ibn Jabal.' Then he narrated from al-Salt ibn Rashid: I asked Tawus about something, and he rebuked me, saying: 'Did this happen?' I said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Allah?' I said: 'Allah.' He said: 'Our companions told us from Mu'adh ibn Jabal that he said: 'O people! Do not hasten the trials before their descent, lest they take you here and there. For if you do not hasten the trials before their descent, the Muslims will not cease to have among them one who, when asked, is guided or who says: 'success.'' Al-Khatib said: 'This is the action of the pious and those who fear for their religion.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of conflicting reports from early scholars regarding the permissibility of asking hypothetical questions. The author reconciles the strict stance of 'Umar (punishing Subaygh) with the permissive practice of other Companions like 'Ali and the Tabi'un. He explains that 'Umar's reaction was directed at questions asked with obstinacy and to cause doubt, not genuine learning. The hadith prohibiting 'ughlutat' (difficult questions) applies to those who seek to confuse and mock scholars. The consensus of the Companions and later scholars affirmed the permissibility of discussing hypothetical rulings, as evidenced by their discussions on fara'id before actual cases. The author concludes that the cautious approach of some early scholars (like Tawus and Mu'adh) was due to personal piety and fear of error, not a general prohibition.

Key Terms
الأُغْلُوْطَات (al-ughlutat): Difficult or tricky questions that are not needed, asked to confuse scholars.
التعنت (al-ta'annut): Obstinacy, asking questions in a hostile or argumentative manner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He asked about a hypothetical that had not occurred.
B) He asked about ambiguous Qur'anic verses with the intent to cause doubt.
C) He rejected a fatwa given by 'Umar.
D) He refused to pay zakat.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits answering hypothetical questions based on consensus, but the mufti must beware of obstinate questioning and assess the questioner's intent.

The Seven Jurists of Medina and Early Fatwa Centers
Historical listing of early fatwa authorities by region

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Seven Jurists of Medina and recall their names along with key scholars from Mecca and Kufa, and explain why knowing these early authorities is essential for a mufti's evaluation of transmitted opinions.

Core Idea
The Seven Jurists of Medina were the primary muftis of the Tabi'un; a mufti must know the chains of authority to evaluate fatwas.

This passage introduces you to the earliest fatwa authorities after the Companions. The 'Seven Jurists of Medina' are a foundational memory anchor for any mufti, but they were not the only scholars. Mecca and Kufa also produced towering figures whose opinions shaped the Hanafi school. Memorizing these names and their centers gives you the geographical and historical map of early Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Seven Jurists via Poetry
Read the two verses carefully. The names are: Ubayd Allah, Urwa, Qasim, Sa'id, Abu Bakr, Sulayman, and Kharijah. Commit them to memory; this is a classic mnemonic used by scholars.
Note the slight variation in the poem: some include Abu Bakr instead of Abu Salama. Know both versions.
2
Identify the Disagreement on Inclusion
The author notes that some scholars include Abu Bakr ibn Abd al-Rahman instead of Abu Salama. Understand that the 'seven' is a conventional number; the core group is agreed upon, but the exact list may vary slightly.
3
Recognize Other Medinan Jurists
After the seven, the text lists other important Medinan muftis: Nafi', Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri, Aban ibn Uthman, Salim, Ali Zayn al-Abidin, Rabi'ah, Abu Ja'far al-Baqir, and Abu al-Zinad. These were also key references for fatwa.
Many of these scholars are frequently cited in later Hanafi works.
4
Learn the Meccan and Kufan Schools
Mecca had Ata' ibn Abi Rabah (a prominent mufti praised by Ibn Abbas), Ali ibn Abi Talha, Mujahid, and Ibn Jurayj. Kufa had Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, al-Sha'bi, Alqama, and others. The Kufan school is particularly important for Hanafi fiqh.
The Hanafi madhhab traces its roots through the Kufan school (Imam Abu Hanifa was a student of Hammad, who studied under Ibrahim al-Nakha'i).
5
Understand the Importance for a Mufti
A mufti relies on transmitted opinions. Knowing the geographical and chronological layers of authorities helps assess the strength of a narration or ruling. For example, a fatwa traced to the Medinan Seven carries weight, but a Hanafi mufti will also prioritize the Kufan chain.
This knowledge is part of usul al-ifta: understanding the hierarchy of sources.

Methodologies of Fatwa Among the Early Muslims
And Kharijah ibn Zaid (may Allah have mercy on them), and they are called the seven jurists. And some have mentioned Abu Bakr ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Harith ibn Hisham (3) among them instead of Abu Salama ibn Abd al-Rahman. Some people have gathered them in poetry:
"Indeed, everyone who does not follow the imams! / His division is unjust, far from the truth. / So take them: Ubayd Allah, Urwa, Qasim, / Sa'id, Abu Bakr, Sulayman, Kharijah!"
And Ibn al-Qayyim mentioned the two verses like this:
"If it is said: Who among knowledge are seven seas? / Their narrations are not outside knowledge. / Then say: They are Ubayd Allah, Urwa, Qasim, / Sa'id, Abu Bakr, Sulayman, Kharijah."

(1) Kharijah ibn Zaid ibn Thabit, the noble companion, the jurist imam son of an imam, and one of the seven prominent jurists, died may Allah have mercy on him in the year 99 AH or 100 AH at age seventy. When Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz (may Allah have mercy on him) learned of his death, he said 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' and clapped one hand on the other and said: 'A breach, by Allah, in Islam.' (Summarized from Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' 4:437-441) (2) Abu Bakr ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Harith ibn Hisham, one of the seven jurists; his father Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Harith was among the senior Tabi'un and nobles of his people. Abu Bakr was born during the caliphate of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) and was called 'the monk of Quraysh' for his abundant prayer. He was blind. He died may Allah have mercy on him in the year 94 AH, which is called the year of the jurists because many of them died in it. And it is said he died in 95 AH. (Summarized from Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' 4:416-419)

Principles of Issuing Fatwa

Methodologies of Fatwa Among the Early Muslims
And among the jurists of Medina besides the aforementioned seven were Nafi', Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri, the judge Yahya ibn Sa'id, Aban ibn Uthman, Salim ibn Abd Allah ibn Umar, Ali ibn al-Husayn Zayn al-Abidin, Rabi'ah ibn Abi Abd al-Rahman, Abu Ja'far al-Baqir, and Abu al-Zinad Abd Allah ibn Dhakwan (may Allah have mercy on them all).
As for Mecca, among those famous from there are Ata' ibn Abi Rabah, Ali ibn Abi Talha, Mujahid ibn Jabr, Amr ibn Dinar, Abd Allah ibn Ubayd Allah ibn Abi Mulayka, Abd al-Malik ibn Jurayj, and others (may Allah have mercy on them).
And in Kufa, famous were Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, Amir ibn Sharahil al-Sha'bi, Alqama, al-Aswad, Murra al-Hamdani, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, and Masruq ibn al-Ajda'.

(1) Aban ibn the Commander of the Faithful Uthman ibn Affan, the imam jurist, the prince; Aban (may Allah have mercy on him) was entrusted with the governorship of Medina for seven years; one of the trustworthy hadith narrators, and among the jurists of Medina who gave fatwa. He suffered a stroke in his later years. He died, may Allah have mercy on him, in the year 105 AH. Summarized from Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' 4:351-353, and al-A'lam 1:27) (2) Salim ibn Abd Allah ibn the Commander of the Faithful Umar ibn al-Khattab, the ascetic imam, the memorizer, one of the seven prominent jurists, Abu Umar and Abu Abd Allah al-Qurashi al-Adawi al-Madani. His birth was during the caliphate of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him). He followed his father in lack of luxury and austerity in living. He died may Allah have mercy on him in 106 AH at the strongest, and it is said 107 AH. He was upon mercy of Allah. (Summarized from Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' 4:457-467) as it is said 108 AH. (3) Ata' ibn Aslam (Abi Rabah), the imam Abu Muhammad al-Qurashi, their client, al-Makki. He was born, may Allah have mercy on him, in Yemen two years after the caliphate of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him), and grew up in Mecca in worship, asceticism, and austerity of life until Ibn Jurayj said: 'The place of prayer was Ata's bed for twenty years.' He was among the best of people in prayer. He was paralyzed. Abu Dawud said: 'His father was Nubian, and he would make baskets.' Ata was one-eyed, withered-handed, flat-nosed, lame, black. He said: 'His hand was cut off with Ibn al-Zubayr.' He was a reference for fatwa, until Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) said: 'O people of Mecca! You gather around me while Ata is with you!' He was a hadith transmitter, and there was some discussion about his mursal narrations mentioned by al-Dhahabi in al-Siyar. He died may Allah have mercy on him in Mecca in 115 AH, and it is said 114 AH. (Summarized from Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' 5:78-88 and al-A'lam 4:235)
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a historical listing of the early authorities of fatwa from the three major centers of Islamic learning: Medina, Mecca, and Kufa. The author begins by naming the famous 'Seven Jurists of Medina' (الفقهاء السبعة), a group of Tabi'un who were the primary muftis after the era of the Companions. The two verses of poetry help memorize their names: Ubayd Allah, Urwa, Qasim, Sa'id, Abu Bakr, Sulayman, and Kharijah. The author then notes a disagreement about whether Abu Salama or Abu Bakr is included, and adds biographies of Kharijah and Abu Bakr in footnotes. Beyond the seven, other notable Medinan jurists are listed, such as Nafi', al-Zuhri, and Aban ibn Uthman. In Mecca, scholars like Ata' ibn Abi Rabah (with a detailed footnote about his attributes) and Ibn Jurayj are mentioned. In Kufa, the school of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and al-Sha'bi is highlighted. This passage serves to inform the student of the primary sources of fiqh transmission—these individuals are the links in the chain of authority. A mufti must know their names and status to properly evaluate legal opinions.

Key Terms
الفقهاء السبعة (al-fuqahā' al-sab'a): The seven renowned jurists of Medina among the Tabi'un who were the primary reference for fatwa after the Companions.
مناهج الفتوى (manāhij al-fatwā): The methods and approaches used by early scholars in issuing fatwas, including reliance on the Qur'an, Hadith, and the opinions of earlier authorities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Seven Jurists of Medina are a memorization anchor, but a mufti must also know the scholarly geography—especially the Kufan school—that underpins the Hanafi tradition.

Adab al-Mufti — Ethics of the Mufti
intermediate
10 min
Salaf's Use of Qiyas: Inheritance and Wine Hadd
Two illustrative examples of qiyas from the Salaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the Salaf used qiyas in deriving the hadd of eighty lashes for wine drinking and evaluate the Hanafi position on this issue.

Core Idea
The Salaf used qiyas to harmonize apparent contradictions in transmitted texts and to derive rulings for new cases.

This lesson examines two examples from the Salaf where analogical reasoning (qiyas) was employed to derive fiqh rulings. The first concerns the inheritance of the grandfather when the brother is present; the second involves the punishment for drinking wine. Both illustrate how early scholars used reasoning to extend or clarify rulings based on transmitted evidence. The wine example is particularly instructive because it shows how qiyas was used not to create a new ruling but to prefer one interpretation over another when the transmitted texts admitted multiple possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples
Read the passage and note the two distinct examples of qiyas: (1) the grandfather and brother both inheriting because they are equally close to the deceased, and (2) the number of lashes for drinking wine, where Ali suggested eighty based on qiyas with slander.
The first example is briefly summarized; the second is detailed.
2
Understand the textual basis for the wine hadd
Recognize that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prescribed forty lashes for wine, but the instrument may have been doubled (a whip with two ends or two sandals). This created uncertainty whether the punishment is forty or eighty.
The author clarifies that the issue was not establishing a new hadd by qiyas, but preferring one of two possible interpretations.
3
Analyze Ali's qiyas argument
Trace the chain: drinking leads to intoxication → intoxication leads to raving → raving leads to slander (qadhf). The hadd for slander is eighty lashes (Quran 24:4). Therefore, by analogy, the hadd for drinking should also be eighty.
This is an argument by analogy (qiyas) where the effective cause (illa) is the resulting slander.
4
Distinguish the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school accepts the reasoning of Ali and the practice of Umar, thus establishing the hadd for wine at eighty lashes. This is the mufta bihi position.
Some early scholars held to forty lashes based on the literal hadith, but the Hanafi view is that the forty were with a doubled instrument.
5
Apply the methodology to similar cases
Consider how this same qiyas method can be used in other areas where the ruling depends on identifying the effective cause. The Salaf's approach demonstrates that qiyas is a valid source of law when the text allows multiple interpretations.
This lesson underscores the importance of understanding the illa in Hanafi usul.

(۲)
وأخرج الإمام مالك رحمه الله تعالى عن ثور بن زيد الديلي أن عمر بن الخطاب رضى الله تعالى عنه استشار في الخمر يشربها الرجل، فقال له على بن أبي طالب رضى الله تعالى عنه: نرى أن تَجْلِده ثمانين، فإنّه إذا شرب سكر، وإذا سكر هذى، وإذا هذى افترى " أو كما قال، فجلد عمر في الخمر ثمانين ) ولم يكن ذلك إثبات الحد بالقياس، وإنما ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلّم ضرب أربعين إما بسوط له طرفان، أو بنعلين، فالقضيّة التي عُرضت على الصحابة هى هل يُعتبر عدد الضرب أربعين، أو ثمانين بالنظر إلى تعدد الآلة. وما ذكره سيدنا على رضى الله تعالى عنه إنما هو ترجيح أحد الاحتمالين بالقياس. وقد أوضحت ذلك في تكملة فتح الملهم بتوفيق الله سبحانه.
ثم إن الصحابة رضى الله تعالى عنهم تفرقوا في البلاد وعلموا أصحابهم، فأخذ عنهم التابعون، وتمسكوا بما أخذوا منهم، فترجح عند كل واحدٍ منهم غالباً ما أخذ من شيخه وأهل بلده من الصحابة رضى الله تعالى عنهم أجمعين. ويقول الشيخ ولى الله الدهلوى رحمه الله تعالى: "وكان سعيد وأصحابه يذهبون إلى أن أهل الحرمين أثبت الناس فى الفقه وأصل مذهبهم فتاوى بن عمر وعائشة وابن عباس وقضايا قُضاة المدينة.. وكان عبد الله إبراهيم وأصحابه يرون أن عبد الله ابن مسعود وأصحابه أثبت الناس في الفقه

أصول الإفتاء
e
مناهج الفتوى في السلف
وأصل مذهبه فتاوى عبد الله بن مسعود وقضايا علي رضي الله تعالى عنهما وفتاواه، وقضايا شريح وغيره من قضاة الكوفة... وكان سعيد بن المُسيَّب لسان فقهاء المدينة، وكان أحفظهم لقضايا عمر بن الخطاب ولحديث أبي هريرة، وإبراهيم لسان فقهاء الكوفة. فإذا تكلّما بشيئ ولم ينسباه إلى أحد، فإنه في الأكثر منسوب إلى أحدٍ من السلف صريحاً أو إيماء ونحو ذلك، فاجتمع عليهما فقهاء بلادهما وأخذوا عنهما وعقلوه وخرَّجوا عليه، والله أعلم.
Then Ja'far remained silent about it. (1) The summary of both examples is that the grandfather and the brother are equal in closeness to the deceased, so he shares in the inheritance. (2) And Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated from Thawr ibn Zaid al-Dili that Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) consulted about wine drunk by a man, and Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'We think you should flog him eighty, because if he drinks he becomes intoxicated, if he becomes intoxicated he raves, if he raves he slanders' — or as he said. So Umar flogged eighty for wine. And that was not establishing the hadd by qiyas, but rather it was established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) as forty lashes, either with a whip with two ends, or with two sandals. So the issue presented to the Companions was whether the number of lashes is forty or eighty in view of the multiplicity of the instrument. And what our master Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) mentioned was only a preference of one of the two probabilities by qiyas. And I have explained that in Takmila Fath al-Mulhim by the grace of Allah. Then the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) dispersed throughout the lands and taught their followers, so the Successors took from them and adhered to what they took from them. Thus, each of them predominantly favored what he took from his shaykh and the people of his town among the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them all). And Shaykh Wali Allah al-Dihlawi (may Allah have mercy on him) says: 'Sa'id and his companions used to hold that the people of the Two Sanctuaries were the most reliable people in fiqh, and the basis of their school is the fatwas of Ibn Umar, Aisha, and Ibn Abbas, and the judgments of the judges of Medina... And Abdullah Ibrahim and his companions used to hold that Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and his companions were the most reliable people in fiqh, and the basis of his school is the fatwas of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, the judgments of Ali (may Allah be pleased with them both) and his fatwas, and the judgments of Shuraih and other judges of Kufa... And Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab was the spokesman of the fuqaha of Medina, and the most retentive of them for the judgments of Umar ibn al-Khattab and the hadith of Abu Huraira; and Ibrahim was the spokesman of the fuqaha of Kufa. If they spoke of something and did not attribute it to anyone, then most often it is attributed to one of the Salaf explicitly or by implication and so on. Thus the fuqaha of their lands agreed upon them, took from them, understood them, and derived rulings based on them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage discusses the methodology of the Salaf (early Muslims) in issuing fatwas, specifically their use of analogical reasoning (qiyas). The author first summarizes two examples (not fully quoted) that demonstrate the equality of the grandfather and brother in proximity to the deceased, implying shared inheritance. He then extensively narrates the well-known incident of Umar consulting about the punishment for drinking wine. Ali suggested eighty lashes, reasoning that drunkenness leads to slander (qadhf), which has a hadd of eighty. However, the author clarifies that this was not establishing a new hadd by qiyas; rather, the original forty lashes from the Prophet was understood to be with a doubled instrument (a whip with two ends or two sandals). The issue was whether the number of lashes should be considered forty or eighty given the multiplicity of the instrument. Ali's opinion was a preference based on qiyas. The passage then describes how after the Companions dispersed, regional schools formed, with Medina relying on the fatwas of Ibn Umar, Aisha, and Ibn Abbas, while Kufa relied on those of Ibn Mas'ud and Ali. This explains the later divergence of the Hanafi and Maliki schools. Shāh Walī Allāh al-Dihlawī's insights are quoted to highlight the authoritative foundations of each region. The overall theme is that legitimate differences in fatwa stem from differing access to prophetic traditions and the reasoning of the Companions.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning: applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause. In this lesson, it is used to prefer the interpretation of eighty lashes.
الحد (al-ḥadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law for certain crimes. Here, the hadd for drinking wine is in question.
العلة (al-illah): The effective cause in qiyas. Here, the illa is that drinking leads to slander, which has a fixed hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Direct revelation from the Prophet
B) Qiyas: drunkenness leads to slander, which has a hadd of eighty
C) Consensus of all Companions
D) A hadith specifically stating eighty lashes

Key Takeaway
The Salaf used qiyas to derive rulings when texts allowed multiple interpretations, and their decisions became the foundation of later madhahib.

Formation of Regional Schools: Medina and Kufa
Historical explanation of regional school formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the dispersion of Companions led to the formation of distinct regional schools, particularly the Medinan and Kufan traditions, and identify the key figures associated with each.

Core Idea
Regional schools emerged from different Companions each region followed, creating diversity within the broader framework of Sunni fiqh.

After the conquests, the Companions dispersed to various lands, teaching their students and establishing local centers of learning. This lesson traces how two major regional schools—Medina and Kufa—developed based on the fatwas and practices of different Companions. The Medinan school relied on the traditions of Umar, Aisha, Ibn Abbas, and others, while the Kufan school (which became the Hanafi school) centered on the teachings of Ibn Mas'ud and Ali. Understanding this historical development is key to appreciating the richness of Islamic legal tradition and the reasons for later madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger: dispersal of Companions
After the Prophet's death and the subsequent conquests, the Companions spread across the Islamic world, each teaching the fiqh they had learned directly.
This geographical dispersion laid the foundation for regional variations in fiqh.
2
List the key Companions for Medina
According to Shah Wali Allah, the Medinan school relied on the fatwas of Ibn Umar, Aisha, and Ibn Abbas, as well as the judgments of the Medinan judges.
Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab was the leading Successor in Medina, known for preserving the judgments of Umar and the hadith of Abu Huraira.
3
List the key Companions for Kufa
The Kufan school (which later contributed to the Hanafi school) followed the fatwas of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the judgments and fatwas of Ali, as well as the judgments of Shuraih and other Kufan judges.
Ibrahim al-Nakha'i was the leading Successor in Kufa, representing the teachings of Ibn Mas'ud and Ali.
4
Understand the role of the Successors (Tabi'un)
The Successors like Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab in Medina and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i in Kufa systematized the fiqh they received from the Companions and taught it to their students, forming the core of the later schools.
They often spoke without attribution, but their statements were usually based on specific Companions.
5
Appreciate the diversity as a mercy
The passage shows that both schools are legitimate and based on sound transmissions. This diversity is not a sign of confusion but a manifestation of the richness of Islamic jurisprudence.
The Hanafi school traces its lineage through Kufa, but it also respects Medinan traditions.

وأصل مذهبه فتاوى عبد الله بن مسعود وقضايا علي رضي الله تعالى عنهما وفتاواه، وقضايا شريح وغيره من قضاة الكوفة... وكان سعيد بن المُسيَّب لسان فقهاء المدينة، وكان أحفظهم لقضايا عمر بن الخطاب ولحديث أبي هريرة، وإبراهيم لسان فقهاء الكوفة. فإذا تكلّما بشيئ ولم ينسباه إلى أحد، فإنه في الأكثر منسوب إلى أحدٍ من السلف صريحاً أو إيماء ونحو ذلك، فاجتمع عليهما فقهاء بلادهما وأخذوا عنهما وعقلوه وخرَّجوا عليه، والله أعلم.
Then Ja'far remained silent about it. (1) The summary of both examples is that the grandfather and the brother are equal in closeness to the deceased, so he shares in the inheritance. (2) And Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated from Thawr ibn Zaid al-Dili that Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) consulted about wine drunk by a man, and Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'We think you should flog him eighty, because if he drinks he becomes intoxicated, if he becomes intoxicated he raves, if he raves he slanders' — or as he said. So Umar flogged eighty for wine. And that was not establishing the hadd by qiyas, but rather it was established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) as forty lashes, either with a whip with two ends, or with two sandals. So the issue presented to the Companions was whether the number of lashes is forty or eighty in view of the multiplicity of the instrument. And what our master Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) mentioned was only a preference of one of the two probabilities by qiyas. And I have explained that in Takmila Fath al-Mulhim by the grace of Allah. Then the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) dispersed throughout the lands and taught their followers, so the Successors took from them and adhered to what they took from them. Thus, each of them predominantly favored what he took from his shaykh and the people of his town among the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them all). And Shaykh Wali Allah al-Dihlawi (may Allah have mercy on him) says: 'Sa'id and his companions used to hold that the people of the Two Sanctuaries were the most reliable people in fiqh, and the basis of their school is the fatwas of Ibn Umar, Aisha, and Ibn Abbas, and the judgments of the judges of Medina... And Abdullah Ibrahim and his companions used to hold that Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and his companions were the most reliable people in fiqh, and the basis of his school is the fatwas of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, the judgments of Ali (may Allah be pleased with them both) and his fatwas, and the judgments of Shuraih and other judges of Kufa... And Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab was the spokesman of the fuqaha of Medina, and the most retentive of them for the judgments of Umar ibn al-Khattab and the hadith of Abu Huraira; and Ibrahim was the spokesman of the fuqaha of Kufa. If they spoke of something and did not attribute it to anyone, then most often it is attributed to one of the Salaf explicitly or by implication and so on. Thus the fuqaha of their lands agreed upon them, took from them, understood them, and derived rulings based on them. And Allah knows best.

Taqlid: Definition and Early Practice
Definition and historical practice of taqlid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define taqlid technically, explain its Quranic basis, and describe how the early generations practiced it without restricting themselves to one scholar.

Core Idea
Taqlid is acting on a scholar's statement without demanding proof, but early Muslims freely consulted multiple scholars.

In this passage, we explore the concept of taqlid—a term often misunderstood. The author grounds it in the Quranic command to ask the people of knowledge, defines it technically, then distinguishes the early practice (flexible consultation) from later adherence to a single madhhab. Understanding this distinction is vital for a mufti to appreciate the historical development of fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic Basis for Asking Scholars
Locate the verse cited (al-Nahl:43) and note that it commands those without knowledge to ask the people of remembrance (ahl al-dhikr). This establishes the obligation of taqlid for non-specialists.
The verse is a dalil for the permissibility and necessity of taqlid.
2
Extract the Technical Definition of Taqlid
Find the phrase 'العمل بقول الغير من غير معرفة دليله أو مطالبته بالدليل' and verify that it means acting on another's statement without knowing or demanding evidence. This is the core definition.
Contrast with blind following: taqlid is based on trust in the scholar's knowledge and piety.
3
Analyze the Early Practice: No Restriction to One Scholar
Read the statement that people in the best centuries did not restrict themselves to a single scholar; they would consult various scholars, though they might have a preferred one based on trust.
This shows that tamadhub (adherence to one madhhab) is a later development.
4
Apply to the Example from Bukhari
Consider the narrated incident: the people of Madinah asked Ibn Abbas a specific question without demanding proof. See how this exemplifies the early practice of taqlid.
The example demonstrates that seeking a fatwa without requiring evidence was normal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسئلة التقليد والتمذهب
كان الناس يستفتون العلماء منذ قديم فى المسائل التي يحتاجون إليها، لأنّ العامة من النّاس لا يستطيعون أن يستنبطوا الأحكام الشرعية من مآخذها الأصيلة، فلابد لهم من الرجوع إلى من عنده معرفة بهذه الأحكام، وهو الذى أمر به الله سبحانه وتعالى فى قوله الكريم: ﴿فَسْتَلُوا أَهْلَ الذِّكْرِ إِن كُنتُمْ لَا تَعْلَمُونَ) [النحل : ٤٣] ومادام المفتى موثوقاً بعلمه وتقواه، فلم يكونوا يُطالبونه بالدليل على ما يقول، وهو معنى التقليد في الاصطلاح، إذ عرفوه بالعمل بقول الغير من غير معرفة دليله أو مطالبته بالدليل. ولكن لم يكونوا في خير القرون يُقيَّدون أنفسهم بالاستفتاء من عالم واحد، بحيث لا يجوزون الاستفتاء من عالم آخر، وإن كان الأفراد لهم مناسبة خاصة بعالم من أهل بلده، فكانت ثقته عليه أكثر من غيره، فيرجع بفضل تلك المناسبة والثقة إلى ذلك العالم في جميع مسائله أوفى معظمها. ومن ذلك ما أخرجه البخارى رحمه الله تعالى عن عكرمة أن أهل المدينة سألوا ابن عباس رضى الله تعالى عنهما هل يجوز للمرأة إذا حاضت أن تَنْفِرَ إلى وطنها بعد طواف الزيارة
The Issue of Taqlid and Adhering to a Madhhab - People have been asking scholars for fatwas since ancient times regarding matters they need, because the general public cannot derive Shari'ah rulings from their original sources. Therefore, they must refer to someone who has knowledge of these rulings, and this is what Allah the Exalted commanded in His noble verse: 'So ask the people of remembrance if you do not know' [al-Nahl: 43]. As long as the mufti is trustworthy in his knowledge and piety, they would not demand from him the evidence for what he says, and this is the meaning of taqlid in technical terminology, as they defined it as 'acting upon the statement of another without knowing his evidence or demanding evidence from him.' However, during the best centuries, they would not restrict themselves to seeking fatwa from one scholar such that they did not permit seeking fatwa from another scholar, even though individuals had a particular connection to a scholar from their town, and their trust in him was greater than in others. So due to that connection and trust, they would refer to that scholar in all or most of their matters. And an example of this is what al-Bukhari (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated from Ikrimah that the people of Madinah asked Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both): 'Is it permissible for a woman who has menstruated to leave for her homeland after Tawaf al-Ziyarah?'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage addresses the concept of taqlid and its historical practice. It begins by establishing the necessity of asking scholars for those lacking the ability to derive rulings independently, citing the Quranic verse commanding those who do not know to ask the people of knowledge. It then defines taqlid technically as acting on a scholar's statement without requiring proof, which was accepted as long as the scholar was trustworthy. Importantly, the author emphasizes that in the early generations (khayr al-qurun), people did not restrict themselves to a single scholar; they would consult various scholars, though they might have a preferred one due to trust or familiarity. The passage ends with an example from Bukhari of the people of Madinah asking Ibn Abbas a specific question about a menstruating woman leaving after Tawaf al-Ziyarah. This illustrates the early practice of asking a scholar without demanding a proof. The implication for usul al-ifta is that taqlid is permissible and necessary for non-mujtahids, but adherence to a specific madhhab (tamadhub) is a later development; early practice allowed consulting multiple scholars.

Key Terms
التقليد (al-taqlid): Acting upon the statement of another without knowing his evidence or demanding evidence from him.
التمذهب (al-tamadhub): Adhering to a specific madhhab, i.e., restricting oneself to the rulings of one school.
المفتى (al-mufti): A qualified scholar who issues fatwas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They strictly prohibited seeking fatwa from more than one scholar.
B) They completely avoided any preference for a particular scholar.
C) They did not restrict themselves to one scholar, though individuals might have a preferred one.
D) They only followed the Prophet's companions and no one else.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is acting on a scholar's statement without proof, and early Muslims freely consulted multiple scholars, a practice that later evolved into adherence to a single madhhab for consistency.

Taqlid and Fatwa-Seeking in the Early Generations
Historical overview of madhhab spread and early taqlid practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define taqlid as practiced by the Salaf, distinguish it from later rigid madhhab adherence, and apply the principle of trust-based fatwa-seeking to a contemporary scenario.

Core Idea
The Salaf followed a scholar due to trust, not blind obligation; they would change their view when evidence emerged.

This lesson explores how the early generations (Salaf) understood taqlid and fatwa. You will learn that taqlid originally meant acting on a scholar's statement without demanding proof, but the Salaf did not bind themselves to a single scholar. Instead, they followed a scholar they trusted and were open to changing their practice when evidence became clear, as shown in the famous story of Ibn Abbas and Zayd ibn Thabit.

Guided Path
1
Define taqlid in the early context
Identify the technical definition of taqlid from the passage: 'acting on the saying of another without knowing his evidence or demanding proof.' Note the Quranic basis (16:43).
2
Analyze the practice of the Salaf
The text states that the Salaf were not bound to consult only one scholar. They sought fatwa from those they trusted most, but did not forbid asking others. This is a key difference from later rigid madhhab affiliation.
3
Examine the story of Ibn Abbas and Zayd ibn Thabit
The people of Madinah rejected Ibn Abbas's fatwa because they trusted Zayd more. When Zayd retracted after learning evidence, they accepted the new ruling. This shows that trust was based on knowledge and piety, not mere stubbornness.
Note that the people did not have to follow Ibn Abbas; their following of Zayd was their own choice.
4
Extract the lesson for muftis and seekers
A mufti should be trustworthy in knowledge and piety. A seeker may choose whom to ask based on trust, but must remain open to evidence. The mufti should not demand blind adherence, nor should the seeker dismiss evidence once it is clarified.
This aligns with the adab al-mufti principle of humbly accepting evidence even if it contradicts one's earlier position.
